1: package com.trulyfreeocr.pipeline;
2: 
3: import java.awt.image.BufferedImage;
4: 
5: import com.trulyfreeocr.model.SegmentedImage;
6: 
7: public class ImageSegmenter {
8: 
9:     private final int tileSize;
10:     private final double percentile;
11:     private final int inpaintRadius;
12: 
13:     public ImageSegmenter() {
14:         this(64, 0.95, 3);
15:     }
16: 
17:     public ImageSegmenter(int tileSize, double percentile, int inpaintRadius) {
18:         this.tileSize = tileSize;
19:         this.percentile = percentile;
20:         this.inpaintRadius = inpaintRadius;
21:     }
22: 
23:     /**
24:      * Orchestrates the full page-segmentation pipeline:
25:      *   grayscale -> background normalization -> Otsu binarization -> inpaint background.
26:      *
27:      * @param image  Input RGB page image (e.g. 300 DPI PDFBox render).
28:      * @return       SegmentedImage holding a binary foreground mask and a cleaned background.
29:      */
30:     public SegmentedImage segment(BufferedImage image) {
31:         int width = image.getWidth();
32:         int height = image.getHeight();
33: 
34:         // Step 1: Convert to grayscale
35:         int[] gray = toGrayscale(image);
36: 
37:         // Step 2: Background normalization
38:         double[] bgNormalized = backgroundNormalize(gray, width, height, tileSize);
39: 
40:         // Step 3: Otsu binarization
41:         int threshold = otsuThreshold(bgNormalized);
42:         BufferedImage foregroundMask = new BufferedImage(width, height, BufferedImage.TYPE_BYTE_BINARY);
43:         for (int y = 0; y < height; y++) {
44:             for (int x = 0; x < width; x++) {
45:                 int idx = y * width + x;
46:                 // Foreground = 0 (black), Background = 1 (white) in binary image
47:                 // Text is dark on light bg, so pixel <= threshold = foreground
48:                 if (gray[idx] <= threshold) {
49:                     foregroundMask.setRGB(x, y, 0xFF000000); // ARGB: black, full opacity
50:                 } else {
51:                     foregroundMask.setRGB(x, y, 0xFFFFFFFF); // ARGB: white, full opacity
52:                 }
53:             }
54:         }
55: 
56:         // Step 4: Cleaned background — fill text regions with surrounding color
57:         BufferedImage cleanedBackground = inpaintBackground(image, foregroundMask, inpaintRadius);
58: 
59:         return new SegmentedImage(foregroundMask, cleanedBackground);
60:     }
61: 
62:     /**
63:      * Converts an RGB BufferedImage to a flat int array of grayscale values
64:      * (0 = black, 255 = white) using the ITU-R BT.601 luma formula.
65:      *
66:      * Magic numbers in luma formula:
67:      *   0.299, 0.587, 0.114 — perception-weighted coefficients for R/G/B.
68:      *   Human vision is most sensitive to green, least to blue.
69:      *   These sum to 1.0, so neutral gray (R=G=B) maps to itself.
70:      *   This is the same formula used in JPEG, NTSC/PAL, and most image libraries.
71:      *
72:      * Bit shifts in RGB extraction:
73:      *   (rgb >> 16) & 0xFF  — extract the red  byte (bits 16-23 of 0xAARRGGBB)
74:      *   (rgb >>  8) & 0xFF  — extract the green byte (bits  8-15)
75:      *   rgb        & 0xFF  — extract the blue byte (bits  0-7)
76:      */
77:     private int[] toGrayscale(BufferedImage image) {
78:         int width = image.getWidth();
79:         int height = image.getHeight();
80:         int[] gray = new int[width * height];
81: 
82:         for (int y = 0; y < height; y++) {
83:             for (int x = 0; x < width; x++) {
84:                 int rgb = image.getRGB(x, y);
85:                 int r = (rgb >> 16) & 0xFF;
86:                 int g = (rgb >> 8) & 0xFF;
87:                 int b = rgb & 0xFF;
88:                 gray[y * width + x] = (int) (0.299 * r + 0.587 * g + 0.114 * b);
89:             }
90:         }
91:         return gray;
92:     }
93: 
94:     /**
95:      * Tile-based background normalization that corrects non-uniform illumination
96:      * (e.g. shadows, gradients, scanner vignetting).
97:      *
98:      * How it works:
99:      *   1. Divide the image into a grid of tiles (default 64x64 px).
100:      *   2. In each tile, estimate the "background level" as the 95th percentile
101:      *      of pixel intensities. Since text is dark on a light background, the
102:      *      bright tail of the histogram represents the page surface (background).
103:      *      The 95th percentile is used (instead of max) to be robust to noise.
104:      *   3. Build a smooth background surface across the page using bilinear
105:      *      interpolation between tile centers.
106:      *   4. Each pixel is then stretched: new_value = old_value * 255 / bg_estimate.
107:      *      Pixels at or near the background level map to 255 (white); dark text
108:      *      pixels stay dark because their value is far below the bg estimate.
109:      *
110:      * Magic numbers:
111:      *   tileSize = 64 — balances accuracy vs performance. 2^6 allows fast
112:      *     integer arithmetic for interpolation (tile centres are at multiples
113:      *     of 64).  Smaller = more detail but slower; larger = faster but coarser.
114:      *   0.95 — 95th percentile.  Picked empirically: the top 5% of brightness
115:      *     values in a tile are assumed to be background, not text or noise.
116:      *   255.0 — maximum 8-bit grayscale value (white).
117:      */
118:     private double[] backgroundNormalize(int[] gray, int width, int height, int tileSize) {
119:         int tilesX = (int) Math.ceil((double) width / tileSize);
120:         int tilesY = (int) Math.ceil((double) height / tileSize);
121: 
122:         // Sample background level per tile (95th percentile = bright, since text is dark)
123:         double[][] bgLevels = new double[tilesY][tilesX];
124:         for (int ty = 0; ty < tilesY; ty++) {
125:             for (int tx = 0; tx < tilesX; tx++) {
126:                 int startX = tx * tileSize;
127:                 int startY = ty * tileSize;
128:                 int endX = Math.min(startX + tileSize, width);
129:                 int endY = Math.min(startY + tileSize, height);
130:                 int count = (endX - startX) * (endY - startY);
131:                 int[] sorted = new int[count];
132:                 int idx = 0;
133:                 for (int y = startY; y < endY; y++) {
134:                     for (int x = startX; x < endX; x++) {
135:                         sorted[idx++] = gray[y * width + x];
136:                     }
137:                 }
138:                 java.util.Arrays.sort(sorted);
139:                 bgLevels[ty][tx] = sorted[(int) (count * percentile)];
140:             }
141:         }
142: 
143:         // Bilinear interpolation of background at each pixel
144:         double[] result = new double[gray.length];
145:         for (int y = 0; y < height; y++) {
146:             for (int x = 0; x < width; x++) {
147:                 float fx = (float) x / tileSize;
148:                 float fy = (float) y / tileSize;
149:                 int tx0 = Math.min((int) Math.floor(fx), tilesX - 1);
150:                 int tx1 = Math.min(tx0 + 1, tilesX - 1);
151:                 int ty0 = Math.min((int) Math.floor(fy), tilesY - 1);
152:                 int ty1 = Math.min(ty0 + 1, tilesY - 1);
153: 
154:                 double bg = bilinearInterp(
155:                         bgLevels[ty0][tx0], bgLevels[ty0][tx1],
156:                         bgLevels[ty1][tx0], bgLevels[ty1][tx1],
157:                         fx - tx0, fy - ty0);
158: 
159:                 int idx = y * width + x;
160:                 if (bg > 0) {
161:                     // Normalize: stretch so that bg -> 255
162:                     result[idx] = Math.min(255.0, gray[idx] * 255.0 / bg);
163:                 } else {
164:                     result[idx] = gray[idx];
165:                 }
166:             }
167:         }
168:         return result;
169:     }
170: 
171:     /**
172:      * Standard bilinear interpolation over a unit square [0,1]x[0,1].
173:      *
174:      *   v00 .. v11  — values at the four corners (top-left, top-right, bottom-left, bottom-right).
175:      *   fx, fy      — fractional offsets within the square (0..1).
176:      *
177:      * Interpolation is performed in two passes:
178:      *   1. Linearly interpolate horizontally between the top corners, and between the bottom corners.
179:      *   2. Linearly interpolate vertically between the two horizontal results.
180:      * This is equivalent to: v = (1-fy)*((1-fx)*v00 + fx*v01) + fy*((1-fx)*v10 + fx*v11)
181:      *
182:      * No magic numbers — pure linear algebra.
183:      */
184:     private double bilinearInterp(double v00, double v01, double v10, double v11, double fx, double fy) {
185:         double top = v00 + (v01 - v00) * fx;
186:         double bot = v10 + (v11 - v10) * fx;
187:         return top + (bot - top) * fy;
188:     }
189: 
190:     /**
191:      * Otsu's method for automatic image thresholding.
192:      *
193:      * Theory: exhaustively search all 256 possible threshold values and pick the
194:      * one that maximises the inter-class variance between foreground and background
195:      * pixel distributions.  This gives optimal separation without any user tuning.
196:      *
197:      * Algorithm steps:
198:      *   1. Build a 256-bin histogram of pixel intensities.
199:      *   2. For each threshold t (0..255), split pixels into two classes:
200:      *      class B (background, intensities > t) and class F (foreground, ≤ t).
201:      *   3. Compute the between-class variance:
202:      *          σ² = wB * wF * (μB - μF)²
203:      *      where w = weight (proportion of pixels), μ = mean intensity.
204:      *   4. Return the t that maximises σ².
205:      *
206:      * Magic numbers:
207:      *   256 — number of bins in an 8-bit grayscale histogram (2^8 possible values).
208:      *   wB, wF  — weights (class pixel counts).  These are integers but the formula
209:      *     uses them as the product to give heavier weight to balanced splits.
210:      */
211:     private int otsuThreshold(double[] pixels) {
212:         int[] histogram = new int[256];
213:         for (double p : pixels) {
214:             int val = Math.min(255, Math.max(0, (int) Math.round(p)));
215:             histogram[val]++;
216:         }
217: 
218:         int total = pixels.length;
219:         double sum = 0;
220:         for (int i = 0; i < 256; i++) {
221:             sum += i * histogram[i];
222:         }
223: 
224:         double sumB = 0;
225:         int wB = 0;
226:         double maxVariance = 0;
227:         int threshold = 0;
228: 
229:         for (int i = 0; i < 256; i++) {
230:             wB += histogram[i];
231:             if (wB == 0) continue;
232:             int wF = total - wB;
233:             if (wF == 0) break;
234: 
235:             sumB += i * histogram[i];
236:             double meanB = sumB / wB;
237:             double meanF = (sum - sumB) / wF;
238:             double variance = (double) wB * wF * (meanB - meanF) * (meanB - meanF);
239: 
240:             if (variance > maxVariance) {
241:                 maxVariance = variance;
242:                 threshold = i;
243:             }
244:         }
245:         return threshold;
246:     }
247: 
248:     /**
249:      * Simple inpainting: replace foreground (text) pixels with the nearest
250:      * background pixel colour found within a search radius.
251:      *
252:      * This produces a "cleaned" page where text regions are filled in with
253:      * the surrounding page colour — useful as a background layer for PDF
254:      * re-assembly (the text will be overlaid as an invisible OCR layer).
255:      *
256:      * Algorithm:
257:      *   For each pixel that the mask identifies as foreground (black):
258:      *     1. Search outward in a square (top-left to bottom-right) within radius.
259:      *     2. Pick the first background (white in mask) pixel found.
260:      *     3. Copy that pixel's colour to the result.
261:      *   Background pixels are copied unchanged.
262:      *
263:      * This is a crude "nearest neighbour" inpaint — a real implementation would
264:      * use a proper diffusion or patch-match algorithm, but this is sufficient
265:      * for the page-background use case.
266:      *
267:      * Magic numbers:
268:      *   radius — search window size (passed in as parameter; typically 3).
269:      *     3 is used because typical text stroke widths at 300 DPI are 2-4 px.
270:      *     Larger values handle thicker strokes but are slower.
271:      *   0xFFFFFF — bitmask to extract only the RGB channels, discarding alpha.
272:      *     setRGB expects full ARGB, so foreground is 0xFF000000 (black w/ alpha=FF)
273:      *     and background is 0xFFFFFFFF (white w/ alpha=FF).
274:      *   Bit shifts for colour reconstruction:
275:      *     (bestR << 16) | (bestG << 8) | bestB — packs R, G, B into 0x00RRGGBB.
276:      *     Alpha is also needed: | 0xFF000000 for full opacity.
277:      *     (nrgb >> 16) & 0xFF, (nrgb >> 8) & 0xFF, nrgb & 0xFF — extract
278:      *     individual 8-bit colour channels from a 32-bit ARGB int.
279:      */
280:     private BufferedImage inpaintBackground(BufferedImage original, BufferedImage mask, int radius) {
281:         int width = original.getWidth();
282:         int height = original.getHeight();
283:         BufferedImage result = new BufferedImage(width, height, BufferedImage.TYPE_INT_RGB);
284: 
285:         for (int y = 0; y < height; y++) {
286:             for (int x = 0; x < width; x++) {
287:                 int maskRgb = mask.getRGB(x, y);
288:                 boolean isForeground = (maskRgb & 0xFFFFFF) == 0;
289: 
290:                 if (!isForeground) {
291:                     result.setRGB(x, y, original.getRGB(x, y));
292:                 } else {
293:                     // Find nearest background pixel within radius
294:                     int bestR = 0, bestG = 0, bestB = 0;
295:                     int found = 0;
296:                     for (int dy = -radius; dy <= radius && found == 0; dy++) {
297:                         for (int dx = -radius; dx <= radius && found == 0; dx++) {
298:                             int nx = x + dx;
299:                             int ny = y + dy;
300:                             if (nx < 0 || nx >= width || ny < 0 || ny >= height) continue;
301:                             int nmask = mask.getRGB(nx, ny);
302:                             if ((nmask & 0xFFFFFF) != 0) {
303:                                 int nrgb = original.getRGB(nx, ny);
304:                                 bestR = (nrgb >> 16) & 0xFF;
305:                                 bestG = (nrgb >> 8) & 0xFF;
306:                                 bestB = nrgb & 0xFF;
307:                                 found++;
308:                             }
309:                         }
310:                     }
311:                     if (found > 0) {
312:                         int rgb = 0xFF000000 | (bestR << 16) | (bestG << 8) | bestB;
313:                         result.setRGB(x, y, rgb);
314:                     } else {
315:                         result.setRGB(x, y, original.getRGB(x, y));
316:                     }
317:                 }
318:             }
319:         }
320:         return result;
321:     }
322: }

(End of file - total 322 lines)
</content>
```

Here's the plan with precise file paths, line numbers, and estimated effort.

---

## Plan: 4 Speed Optimizations

### 1. Bulk `getRGB`/`setRGB` — `ImageSegmenter.java`

**Problem:** Three pixel loops call per-pixel `getRGB(x, y)`/`setRGB(x, y, ...)` through Java's `ColorModel` JNI translation layer — 6-8× slower than bulk array ops.

**Changes:**

| Method | Lines | Current | Replace with |
|--------|-------|---------|-------------|
| `toGrayscale()` | 77-92 | Per-pixel `getRGB(x,y)` + luma | `image.getRGB(0,0,w,h,pixels,0,w)` once, then loop over `pixels[]` |
| `segment()` | 43-54 | Per-pixel `setRGB(x,y,0xFF....)` | Build `int[] maskPixels`, then `foregroundMask.setRGB(0,0,w,h,maskPixels,0,w)` once |
| `inpaintBackground()` | 280-321 | Per-pixel `getRGB/setRGB` on `original` and `mask` | Bulk-read `original` and `mask` into `int[] origPixels`, `int[] maskPixels`; write `result.setRGB(0,0,w,h,resultPixels,0,w)` once. Inner search loop indexes into `maskPixels[]` directly (avoids repeated `getRGB` for the 49-neighbor search) |

**Side effect:** `inpaintBackground()` currently takes `BufferedImage mask` but calls `mask.getRGB(nx, ny)` in the inner search loop. With bulk read, the inner loop just checks `maskPixels[ny * w + nx]` — no method call overhead.

**Effort:** ~30 lines changed, ~5 lines added. Straightforward refactor — zero logic change.

---

### 2. PNG → BMP — 3 files

**Problem:** `ImageIO.write(..., "png", ...)` runs Java's single-threaded Deflate compressor per page (4×/page). Tesseract and jbig2enc auto-detect image format, so no subprocess changes needed.

**Changes:**

| File | Line(s) | Change |
|------|---------|--------|
| `TrulyFreeOCR.java` | 175, 186, 192 | `ImageIO.write(..., "bmp", ...)` + rename to `*.bmp` |
| `JBIG2Compressor.java` | 193, 104-105 | `ImageIO.write(..., "bmp", ...)` + rename to `*.bmp` |
| `OCREngine.java` | 46 | Read `page-{i}.bmp` instead of `page-{i}.png` |

**Tradeoff:** BMP is uncompressed, so temp files grow from ~1 MB to ~8 MB per page. At 100 pages that's ~800 MB vs ~100 MB on disk. TIFF with LZW might be a middle ground — similar compression to PNG but faster.

**Question:** Do you want bare BMP (fastest writes, largest disk), or TIFF with LZW compression (slightly slower writes, smaller files)? The reviewer suggested both.

**Effort:** ~6 lines changed across 3 files.

---

### 3. Page-level multithreading — `TrulyFreeOCR.java`

**Problem:** Both passes are plain `for` loops. The `--threads` CLI option exists at line 69 but is never read.

**Changes:**

1. **Resurrect the `threads` field** — pass it into `runPipeline()`, compute worker count:
   ```java
   int workerThreads = threads != null ? threads
       : settings.getInt("pipeline.ocr.maxThreads", 1);
   ```

2. **Pass 1 parallelism** — Replace the `for (int i = 0; i < pageCount; i++)` loop with an `ExecutorService` that submits pages to a fixed thread pool. Each page task:
   - Renders page `i` (PDFRenderer is not thread-safe — need `synchronized` or per-thread `PDFRenderer`)
   - Segments and OCRs
   - Saves PNGs/TSV to disk (results are per-page, no shared mutable state)

3. **Pass 2 parallelism** — Less beneficial (assembly is I/O-light), but same pattern: submit pages to thread pool, each loads background from disk and calls `addPage()`.

4. **OCR subprocess contention** — Tesseract is memory-hungry and I/O-intensive. Cap the thread pool at `max(1, min(workerThreads, availableProcessors()))` and keep the ExeutorService bounded. The 120s timeout already handles stalled subprocesses.

5. **Important: PDFRenderer is NOT thread-safe.** Need to either:
   - Wrap `renderer.renderImageWithDPI(i, dpi)` in a `synchronized` block (simplest, bottleneck on render)
   - Create a `PDFRenderer` per thread from the shared `PDDocument` (PDFBox docs say renderer is not thread-safe, but creating per-thread may work)
   - Pre-render all pages sequentially (no parallelism on rendering), then parallelize segmentation + OCR

**Recommendation:** Start with parallelizing only segmentation + OCR (not rendering), since rendering is fast (~2s/page) compared to segmentation (~12s) + OCR (~35s). This avoids the PDFRenderer thread-safety issue entirely.

**Effort:** ~40 lines added. The rendering remains sequential; `for` loop body submits a `Callable` to `ExecutorService.invokeAll()`. Results collected in order via `Future.get()`.

---

### 4. Otsu `double[]` → `int[]` — `ImageSegmenter.java`

**Problem:** `backgroundNormalize()` returns `double[]`, but `otsuThreshold()` immediately bins into a 256-entry `int[]` histogram via `Math.round()/min/max` per pixel. The double precision is unused — the output is always clamped to `[0, 255]`.

**Changes:**

| Method | Lines | Change |
|--------|-------|--------|
| `backgroundNormalize()` | 118-169 | Return `int[]` instead of `double[]`. Line 162: `result[idx] = (int) Math.min(255.0, gray[idx] * 255.0 / bg)` instead of `Math.min(255.0, ...)` into `double[]` |
| `otsuThreshold()` | 211-246 | Accept `int[]` instead of `double[]`. Line 213-214: `for (int p : pixels) histogram[p]++;` — no rounding, no clamping needed (already `0..255`) |
| `segment()` | 38-41 | `int[] bgNormalized = backgroundNormalize(...)` and `int threshold = otsuThreshold(bgNormalized)` |

**Effort:** ~6 lines changed.

---

### Order & Dependencies

1. **#4 first** (Otsu `int[]`) — trivial, no risk
2. **#1 next** (bulk `getRGB`) — bigger change, independent of #4
3. **#2 next** (BMP) — simple mechanical change
4. **#3 last** (threading) — most complex, builds on the streaming foundation already in place

### Estimated total speedup

| Pages | Current | After #1+#2+#4 | After #3 (2 workers) |
|-------|---------|--------------|---------------------|
| 1 | 85s | 70-72s | 70-72s (−18%) |
| 100 | 8500s (2.4h) | 7000s (1.9h) | ~4200s (1.2h) **−51%** |

The threading improvement is the multiplier: for multi-page documents it's the difference between hours and under-an-hour.

Want me to refine any part of this, or pick a starting point?

3: import java.awt.image.BufferedImage;

5: import com.trulyfreeocr.model.SegmentedImage;

7: public class ImageSegmenter {
8: 
9:     private final int tileSize;
10:     private final double percentile;
11:     private final int inpaintRadius;
12: 
13:     public ImageSegmenter() {
14:         this(64, 0.95, 3);
15:     }
16: 
17:     public ImageSegmenter(int tileSize, double percentile, int inpaintRadius) {
18:         this.tileSize = tileSize;
19:         this.percentile = percentile;
20:         this.inpaintRadius = inpaintRadius;
21:     }
22: 
23:     /**
24:      * Orchestrates the full page-segmentation pipeline:
25:      *   grayscale -> background normalization -> Otsu binarization -> inpaint background.
26:      *
27:      * @param image  Input RGB page image (e.g. 300 DPI PDFBox render).
28:      * @return       SegmentedImage holding a binary foreground mask and a cleaned background.
29:      */
30:     public SegmentedImage segment(BufferedImage image) {
31:         int width = image.getWidth();
32:         int height = image.getHeight();
33: 
34:         // Step 1: Convert to grayscale
35:         int[] gray = toGrayscale(image);
36: 
37:         // Step 2: Background normalization
38:         int[] bgNormalized = backgroundNormalize(gray, width, height, tileSize);
39: 
40:         // Step 3: Otsu binarization
41:         int threshold = otsuThreshold(bgNormalized);
42:         BufferedImage foregroundMask = new BufferedImage(width, height, BufferedImage.TYPE_BYTE_BINARY);
43:         for (int y = 0; y < height; y++) {
44:             for (int x = 0; x < width; x++) {
45:                 int idx = y * width + x;
46:                 // Foreground = 0 (black), Background = 1 (white) in binary image
47:                 // Text is dark on light bg, so pixel <= threshold = foreground
48:                 if (gray[idx] <= threshold) {
49:                     foregroundMask.setRGB(x, y, 0xFF000000); // ARGB: black, full opacity
50:                 } else {
51:                     foregroundMask.setRGB(x, y, 0xFFFFFFFF); // ARGB: white, full opacity
52:                 }
53:             }
54:         }
55: 
56:         // Step 4: Cleaned background — fill text regions with surrounding color
57:         BufferedImage cleanedBackground = inpaintBackground(image, foregroundMask, inpaintRadius);
58: 
59:         return new SegmentedImage(foregroundMask, cleanedBackground);
60:     }
61: 
62:     /**
63:      * Converts an RGB BufferedImage to a flat int array of grayscale values
64:      * (0 = black, 255 = white) using the ITU-R BT.601 luma formula.
65:      *
66:      * Magic numbers in luma formula:
67:      *   0.299, 0.587, 0.114 — perception-weighted coefficients for R/G/B.
68:      *   Human vision is most sensitive to green, least to blue.
69:      *   These sum to 1.0, so neutral gray (R=G=B) maps to itself.
70:      *   This is the same formula used in JPEG, NTSC/PAL, and most image libraries.
71:      *
72:      * Bit shifts in RGB extraction:
73:      *   (rgb >> 16) & 0xFF  — extract the red  byte (bits 16-23 of 0xAARRGGBB)
74:      *   (rgb >>  8) & 0xFF  — extract the green byte (bits  8-15)
75:      *   rgb        & 0xFF  — extract the blue byte (bits  0-7)
76:      */
77:     private int[] toGrayscale(BufferedImage image) {
78:         int width = image.getWidth();
79:         int height = image.getHeight();
80:         int[] gray = new int[width * height];
81: 
82:         for (int y = 0; y < height; y++) {
83:             for (int x = 0; x < width; x++) {
84:                 int rgb = image.getRGB(x, y);
85:                 int r = (rgb >> 16) & 0xFF;
86:                 int g = (rgb >> 8) & 0xFF;
87:                 int b = rgb & 0xFF;
88:                 gray[y * width + x] = (int) (0.299 * r + 0.587 * g + 0.114 * b);
89:             }
90:         }
91:         return gray;
92:     }
93: 
94:     /**
95:      * Tile-based background normalization that corrects non-uniform illumination
96:      * (e.g. shadows, gradients, scanner vignetting).
97:      *
98:      * How it works:
99:      *   1. Divide the image into a grid of tiles (default 64x64 px).
100:      *   2. In each tile, estimate the "background level" as the 95th percentile
101:      *      of pixel intensities. Since text is dark on a light background, the
102:      *      bright tail of the histogram represents the page surface (background).
103:      *      The 95th percentile is used (instead of max) to be robust to noise.
104:      *   3. Build a smooth background surface across the page using bilinear
105:      *      interpolation between tile centers.
106:      *   4. Each pixel is then stretched: new_value = old_value * 255 / bg_estimate.
107:      *      Pixels at or near the background level map to 255 (white); dark text
108:      *      pixels stay dark because their value is far below the bg estimate.
109:      *
110:      * Magic numbers:
111:      *   tileSize = 64 — balances accuracy vs performance. 2^6 allows fast
112:      *     integer arithmetic for interpolation (tile centres are at multiples
113:      *     of 64).  Smaller = more detail but slower; larger = faster but coarser.
114:      *   0.95 — 95th percentile.  Picked empirically: the top 5% of brightness
115:      *     values in a tile are assumed to be background, not text or noise.
116:      *   255.0 — maximum 8-bit grayscale value (white).
117:      */
118:     private int[] backgroundNormalize(int[] gray, int width, int height, int tileSize) {
119:         int tilesX = (int) Math.ceil((double) width / tileSize);
120:         int tilesY = (int) Math.ceil((double) height / tileSize);
121: 
122:         // Sample background level per tile (95th percentile = bright, since text is dark)
123:         double[][] bgLevels = new double[tilesY][tilesX];
124:         for (int ty = 0; ty < tilesY; ty++) {
125:             for (int tx = 0; tx < tilesX; tx++) {
126:                 int startX = tx * tileSize;
127:                 int startY = ty * tileSize;
128:                 int endX = Math.min(startX + tileSize, width);
129:                 int endY = Math.min(startY + tileSize, height);
130:                 int count = (endX - startX) * (endY - startY);
131:                 int[] sorted = new int[count];
132:                 int idx = 0;
133:                 for (int y = startY; y < endY; y++) {
134:                     for (int x = startX; x < endX; x++) {
135:                         sorted[idx++] = gray[y * width + x];
136:                     }
137:                 }
138:                 java.util.Arrays.sort(sorted);
139:                 bgLevels[ty][tx] = sorted[(int) (count * percentile)];
140:             }
141:         }
142: 
143:         // Bilinear interpolation of background at each pixel
144:         int[] result = new int[gray.length];
145:         for (int y = 0; y < height; y++) {
146:             for (int x = 0; x < width; x++) {
147:                 float fx = (float) x / tileSize;
148:                 float fy = (float) y / tileSize;
149:                 int tx0 = Math.min((int) Math.floor(fx), tilesX - 1);
150:                 int tx1 = Math.min(tx0 + 1, tilesX - 1);
151:                 int ty0 = Math.min((int) Math.floor(fy), tilesY - 1);
152:                 int ty1 = Math.min(ty0 + 1, tilesY - 1);
153: 
154:                 double bg = bilinearInterp(
155:                         bgLevels[ty0][tx0], bgLevels[ty0][tx1],
156:                         bgLevels[ty1][tx0], bgLevels[ty1][tx1],
157:                         fx - tx0, fy - ty0);
158: 
159:                 int idx = y * width + x;
160:                 if (bg > 0) {
161:                     // Normalize: stretch so that bg -> 255
162:                     result[idx] = (int) Math.min(255.0, gray[idx] * 255.0 / bg);
163:                 } else {
164:                     result[idx] = gray[idx];
165:                 }
166:             }
167:         }
168:         return result;
169:     }
170: 
171:     /**
172:      * Standard bilinear interpolation over a unit square [0,1]x[0,1].
173:      *
174:      *   v00 .. v11  — values at the four corners (top-left, top-right, bottom-left, bottom-right).
175:      *   fx, fy      — fractional offsets within the square (0..1).
176:      *
177:      * Interpolation is performed in two passes:
178:      *   1. Linearly interpolate horizontally between the top corners, and between the bottom corners.
179:      *   2. Linearly interpolate vertically between the two horizontal results.
180:      * This is equivalent to: v = (1-fy)*((1-fx)*v00 + fx*v01) + fy*((1-fx)*v10 + fx*v11)
181:      *
182:      * No magic numbers — pure linear algebra.
183:      */
184:     private double bilinearInterp(double v00, double v01, double v10, double v11, double fx, double fy) {
185:         double top = v00 + (v01 - v00) * fx;
186:         double bot = v10 + (v11 - v10) * fx;
187:         return top + (bot - top) * fy;
188:     }
189: 
190:     /**
191:      * Otsu's method for automatic image thresholding.
192:      *
193:      * Theory: exhaustively search all 256 possible threshold values and pick the
194:      * one that maximises the inter-class variance between foreground and background
195:      * pixel distributions.  This gives optimal separation without any user tuning.
196:      *
197:      * Algorithm steps:
198:      *   1. Build a 256-bin histogram of pixel intensities.
199:      *   2. For each threshold t (0..255), split pixels into two classes:
200:      *      class B (background, intensities > t) and class F (foreground, ≤ t).
201:      *   3. Compute the between-class variance:
202:      *          σ² = wB * wF * (μB - μF)²
203:      *      where w = weight (proportion of pixels), μ = mean intensity.
204:      *   4. Return the t that maximises σ².
205:      *
206:      * Magic numbers:
207:      *   256 — number of bins in an 8-bit grayscale histogram (2^8 possible values).
208:      *   wB, wF  — weights (class pixel counts).  These are integers but the formula
209:      *     uses them as the product to give heavier weight to balanced splits.
210:      */
211:     private int otsuThreshold(int[] pixels) {
212:         int[] histogram = new int[256];
213:         for (int p : pixels) {
214:             histogram[p]++;
215:         }
216: 
217:         int total = pixels.length;
218:         double sum = 0;
219:         for (int i = 0; i < 256; i++) {
220:             sum += i * histogram[i];
221:         }
222: 
223:         double sumB = 0;
224:         int wB = 0;
225:         double maxVariance = 0;
226:         int threshold = 0;
227: 
228:         for (int i = 0; i < 256; i++) {
229:             wB += histogram[i];
230:             if (wB == 0) continue;
231:             int wF = total - wB;
232:             if (wF == 0) break;
233: 
234:             sumB += i * histogram[i];
235:             double meanB = sumB / wB;
236:             double meanF = (sum - sumB) / wF;
237:             double variance = (double) wB * wF * (meanB - meanF) * (meanB - meanF);
238: 
239:             if (variance > maxVariance) {
240:                 maxVariance = variance;
241:                 threshold = i;
242:             }
243:         }
244:         return threshold;
245:     }
246: 
247:     /**
248:      * Simple inpainting: replace foreground (text) pixels with the nearest
249:      * background pixel colour found within a search radius.
250:      *
251:      * This produces a "cleaned" page where text regions are filled in with
252:      * the surrounding page colour — useful as a background layer for PDF
253:      * re-assembly (the text will be overlaid as an invisible OCR layer).
254:      *
255:      * Algorithm:
256:      *   For each pixel that the mask identifies as foreground (black):
257:      *     1. Search outward in a square (top-left to bottom-right) within radius.
258:      *     2. Pick the first background (white in mask) pixel found.
259:      *     3. Copy that pixel's colour to the result.
260:      *   Background pixels are copied unchanged.
261:      *
262:      * This is a crude "nearest neighbour" inpaint — a real implementation would
263:      * use a proper diffusion or patch-match algorithm, but this is sufficient
264:      * for the page-background use case.
265:      *
266:      * Magic numbers:
267:      *   radius — search window size (passed in as parameter; typically 3).
268:      *     3 is used because typical text stroke widths at 300 DPI are 2-4 px.
269:      *     Larger values handle thicker strokes but are slower.
270:      *   0xFFFFFF — bitmask to extract only the RGB channels, discarding alpha.
271:      *     setRGB expects full ARGB, so foreground is 0xFF000000 (black w/ alpha=FF)
272:      *     and background is 0xFFFFFFFF (white w/ alpha=FF).
273:      *   Bit shifts for colour reconstruction:
274:      *     (bestR << 16) | (bestG << 8) | bestB — packs R, G, B into 0x00RRGGBB.
275:      *     Alpha is also needed: | 0xFF000000 for full opacity.
276:      *     (nrgb >> 16) & 0xFF, (nrgb >> 8) & 0xFF, nrgb & 0xFF — extract
277:      *     individual 8-bit colour channels from a 32-bit ARGB int.
278:      */
279:     private BufferedImage inpaintBackground(BufferedImage original, BufferedImage mask, int radius) {
280:         int width = original.getWidth();
281:         int height = original.getHeight();
282:         BufferedImage result = new BufferedImage(width, height, BufferedImage.TYPE_INT_RGB);
283: 
284:         for (int y = 0; y < height; y++) {
285:             for (int x = 0; x < width; x++) {
286:                 int maskRgb = mask.getRGB(x, y);
287:                 boolean isForeground = (maskRgb & 0xFFFFFF) == 0;
288: 
289:                 if (!isForeground) {
290:                     result.setRGB(x, y, original.getRGB(x, y));
291:                 } else {
292:                     // Find nearest background pixel within radius
293:                     int bestR = 0, bestG = 0, bestB = 0;
294:                     int found = 0;
295:                     for (int dy = -radius; dy <= radius && found == 0; dy++) {
296:                         for (int dx = -radius; dx <= radius && found == 0; dx++) {
297:                             int nx = x + dx;
298:                             int ny = y + dy;
299:                             if (nx < 0 || nx >= width || ny < 0 || ny >= height) continue;
300:                             int nmask = mask.getRGB(nx, ny);
301:                             if ((nmask & 0xFFFFFF) != 0) {
302:                                 int nrgb = original.getRGB(nx, ny);
303:                                 bestR = (nrgb >> 16) & 0xFF;
304:                                 bestG = (nrgb >> 8) & 0xFF;
305:                                 bestB = nrgb & 0xFF;
306:                                 found++;
307:                             }
308:                         }
309:                     }
310:                     if (found > 0) {
311:                         int rgb = 0xFF000000 | (bestR << 16) | (bestG << 8) | bestB;
312:                         result.setRGB(x, y, rgb);
313:                     } else {
314:                         result.setRGB(x, y, original.getRGB(x, y));
315:                     }
316:                 }
317:             }
318:         }
319:         return result;
320:     }
321: }

(End of file - total 321 lines)

2. Operate on flat arrays in the nested loops (access by `[y * width + x]`)

The tricky part: the inner search loop accesses `mask.getRGB(nx, ny)` and `original.getRGB(nx, ny)` — these become `maskPixels[ny * width + nx]` and `origPixels[ny * width + nx]`.

But changing the method signature would be cleaner. Let me change `inpaintBackground` to accept `int[] maskPixels` instead of `BufferedImage mask`.

Actually, let me look at how it's called:

```java
BufferedImage cleanedBackground = inpaintBackground(image, foregroundMask, inpaintRadius);
```

If I change the signature, I need to pass the pixel array. But `maskPixels` is local to `segment()`. I could pass both the mask `int[]` array and the original `BufferedImage` — or better yet, pass the original's pixel array too.

Actually, the cleanest approach: change `inpaintBackground` to accept `int[] origPixels, int[] maskPixels, int width, int height, int radius` and return `BufferedImage`. Then the method creates the result BufferedImage, builds the output `int[]`, and bulk-writes it.

  "filePath": "/home/bgu/GitHub/msmarkgu/TrulyFreeOCR/src/main/java/com/trulyfreeocr/pipeline/ImageSegmenter.java",

dy <= radius && found == 0; dy++) {\n                        for (int dx = -radius; dx <= radius && found == 0; dx++) {\n                            int nx = x + dx;\n                            int ny = y + dy;\n                            if (nx < 0 || nx >= width || ny < 0 || ny >= height) continue;\n                            int nmask = mask.getRGB(nx, ny);\n                            if ((nmask & 0xFFFFFF) != 0) {\n                                int nrgb = original.getRGB(nx, ny);\n                                bestR = (nrgb >> 16) & 0xFF;\n                                bestG = (nrgb >> 8) & 0xFF;\n                                bestB = nrgb & 0xFF;\n                                found++;\n                            }\n                        }\n                    }\n                    if (found > 0) {\n                        int rgb = 0xFF000000 | (bestR << 16) | (bestG << 8) | bestB;\n                        result.setRGB(x, y, rgb);\n                    } else {\n                        result.setRGB(x, y, original.getRGB(x, y));\n                    }\n                }\n            }\n        }\n        return result;\n    }",
  "newString": "    private BufferedImage inpaintBackground(int[] origPixels, int[] maskPixels, int width, int height, int radius) {\n        int[] resultPixels = new int[width * height];\n\n        for (int y = 0; y < height; y++) {\n            for (int x = 0; x < width; x++) {\n                int idx = y * width + x;\n                boolean isForeground = (maskPixels[idx] & 0xFFFFFF) == 0;\n\n                if (!isForeground) {\n                    resultPixels[idx] = origPixels[idx];\n                } else {\n                    // Find nearest background pixel within radius\n                    int bestR = 0, bestG = 0, bestB = 0;\n                    int found = 0;\n                    for (int dy = -radius; dy <= radius && found == 0; dy++) {\n                        for (int dx = -radius; dx <= radius && found == 0; dx++) {\n                            int nx = x + dx;\n                            int ny = y + dy;\n                            if (nx < 0 || nx >= width || ny < 0 || ny >= height) continue;\n                            int nmask = maskPixels[ny * width + nx];\n                            if ((nmask & 0xFFFFFF) != 0) {\n                                int nrgb = origPixels[ny * width + nx];\n                                bestR = (nrgb >> 16) & 0xFF;\n                                bestG = (nrgb >> 8) & 0xFF;\n                                bestB = nrgb & 0xFF;\n                                found++;\n                            }\n                        }\n                    }\n                    if (found > 0) {\n                        resultPixels[idx] = 0xFF000000 | (bestR << 16) | (bestG << 8) | bestB;\n                    } else {\n                        resultPixels[idx] = origPixels[idx];\n                    }\n                }\n            }\n        }\n\n        BufferedImage result = new BufferedImage(width, height, BufferedImage.TYPE_INT_RGB);\n        result.setRGB(0, 0, width, height, resultPixels, 0, width);\n        return result;\n    }"
}
```

<details>
<summary>Thinking</summary>

Now #2 — PNG → BMP. I need to change 3 files:

1. **TrulyFreeOCR.java** — 3 `ImageIO.write(..., "png", ...)` calls (lines 175, 186, 192)
2. **OCREngine.java** — reads `page-{i}.png` (line 46), also removes `.png` for prefix (line 52)
3. **JBIG2Compressor.java** — writes temp PNG (line 193), batch mode constructs `mask-{i}.png` paths (lines 104-105)

For BMP:
- BMP is uncompressed, fast writes
- Extension: `.bmp`
- Tesseract and jbig2enc auto-detect format — no subprocess changes needed
- OCREngine reads the image to get dimensions (line 47-48 via `ImageIO.read`) — this works with BMP
- OCREngine constructs Tesseract command with the file path — Tesseract auto-detects format
- JBIG2Compressor batch reads `mask-{i}.png` — need to change to `.bmp`

Let me make all the changes:

</details>

All 39 tests pass. Now I need to tackle #3 — page-level multithreading.

The plan:
1. Wire the `--threads` CLI option into `runPipeline()`
2. Pass 1: Replace sequential `for` with thread pool, parallelizing segmentation + OCR (rendering stays sequential to avoid PDFRenderer thread-safety issues)
3. Pass 2: Also parallelize assembly (each page is independent)

The tricky parts:
- PDFRenderer is not thread-safe. I'll render pages sequentially before the thread pool.
- OCR subprocesses must be capped to avoid CPU thrashing.
- The threading is applied to Pass 1 (segment + OCR) and Pass 2 (assemble), each page is independent.

Actually, looking at the current code more carefully, the plan said to keep rendering sequential. But looking at the code flow:

```java
for (int i = 0; i < pageCount; i++) {
    BufferedImage page = renderer.renderImageWithDPI(i, dpi);  // sequential render
    BufferedImage gray = toGrayscale(page);
    ImageIO.write(gray, "bmp", ...);                           // save for OCR
    
    // segment
    // save bg, mask
    // release page images
    
    // OCR (reads from disk)
    PageResult ocr = ocrEngine.ocr(i, tempDir);
    ocrResults.add(ocr);
}
```

For parallelization, I can:
1. Render sequentially, put the rendered images into a queue/array
2. Submit to thread pool: for each page, segment + save files + OCR
3. Collect results

But rendering all pages first and storing them in an array would break the OOM streaming. So a better approach:
- Use a producer-consumer pattern: render one page at a time (sequential), submit segment+OCR task to thread pool
- The rendering stays sequential, but segmentation and OCR happen in parallel with rendering of other pages

Actually, the simplest approach that doesn't break OOM safety:
- Sequential render → toGrayscale → save grayscale BMP → segment → save mask BMP → save bg BMP → submit OCR to executor
- Use a completion service to collect results in order

Wait, but that still runs segmentation and OCR per page, just OCR might run in parallel with the next page's segmentation. The segmentation is lightweight (~12s) compared to OCR (~35s), so overlapping OCR with the next page's render+segment would help.

Let me think about the simplest implementation:
1. Create an `ExecutorService` with `workerThreads` threads
2. In Pass 1: for each page, render + grayscale + save page BMP + segment + save bg/mask BMPs → submit OCR as a `Callable<PageResult>` to the executor
3. Collect futures in order
4. In Pass 2: submit assembly tasks to the same executor

Actually, the simplest approach that avoids complex producer-consumer:
- Collect all page image data in memory first? No, OOM.
- Just parallelize the OCR calls, since that's the heaviest part (35s).

Actually, simplest is to use an `ExecutorService` and submit each page as a task. The render + segment + save part is fast (~15s total), and OCR is slow (~35s). If we have 2 threads, page 0 starts OCR while page 1 renders/segments. The total time for N pages would be roughly `max(renderSegment, OCR_per_page * pages / threads)`.

Let me implement this step by step.

For Pass 1:
- Sequential loop for render + grayscale + segment + save files
- Submit OCR task to executor
- Collect results

But there's a subtlety: the render + segment + save is about 15s per page, and OCR is 35s per page. With 2 threads:
- Page 0: render+segment (15s), then OCR starts (35s)
- Page 1: render+segment starts immediately after page 0's render+segment (15s), OCR starts at 30s
- Both OCRs run in parallel from 30s to 50s (20s overlap)

So the first page takes 50s, remaining pages at 15s interval each (render+segment while previous OCR runs). Total for N pages: `15 + max(OCR_time, (N-1) * renderSegment_time)` ≈ 50s for 2 pages, but grows linearly after that.

OK, I think the implementation is straightforward. Let me write the code.

Actually, looking at this more carefully, I need to be careful about the structure. The current Pass 1 also builds the `ocrResults` list in order. With futures, I need to maintain order.

Here's my plan:

```java
int workerThreads = Math.max(1, resolveThreads());
ExecutorService executor = Executors.newFixedThreadPool(workerThreads);
List<Future<PageResult>> futures = new ArrayList<>(pageCount);

// Pass 1
for (int i = 0; i < pageCount; i++) {
    // render + segment + save files (still sequential)
    ...
    
    // Submit OCR to thread pool
    final int pageIdx = i;
    futures.add(executor.submit(() -> ocrEngine.ocr(pageIdx, tempDir)));
}

// Collect results in order
for (Future<PageResult> f : futures) {
    ocrResults.add(f.get());
}
```

For Pass 2, similar pattern:
```java
// Pass 2
List<Future<PDPage>> assemblyFutures = new ArrayList<>(pageCount);
for (int i = 0; i < pageCount; i++) {
    final int pageIdx = i;
    assemblyFutures.add(executor.submit(() -> {
        BufferedImage bg = ImageIO.read(new File(tempDir, "bg-" + pageIdx + ".bmp"));
        PDPage outPage;
        if (jbig2Batch != null && jbig2Batch.getGlobalSym().length > 0) {
            // JBIG2 path
            JBIG2Compressor.CompressionResult pageData = jbig2Batch.getPages().get(pageIdx);
            outPage = assembler.addPageJbig2(output, source, pageIdx, bg, ...);
        } else if (useMrc) { ... }
        else { ... }
        bg = null;
        return outPage;
    }));
}

for (Future<PDPage> f : assemblyFutures) {
    outPages.add(f.get());
}
```

Wait, Pass 2 calls `addPageJbig2(output, source, ...)` which modifies `output` PDDocument. Multiple threads modifying the same `PDDocument` is not safe. PDFBox's `PDDocument.addPage()` is not thread-safe.

So Pass 2 must be sequential. Only Pass 1 can be parallelized (OCR subprocesses don't share state).

Actually, looking at Pass 2 more carefully: it reads a file from disk, calls `assembler.addPage()` or `assembler.addPageJbig2()`, which adds a page to `output`. Two threads adding pages to the same PDDocument would corrupt it. So Pass 2 must stay sequential.

But wait — I could create separate PDDocuments per page in Pass 2, then merge them in `finishAssembly`. But that's complex and doesn't help much since Pass 2 is fast (~5s/page vs 35s for OCR).

So the plan:
- Pass 1: Parallelize OCR only (render+segment stays sequential since they're fast and share state)
- Pass 2: Keep sequential

This is cleaner and avoids all the thread-safety issues.

```java
int segThreads = Runtime.getRuntime().availableProcessors();

int resolvedThreads = threads != null ? threads : ocrMax;
```

```

```java
int workerCount = threads != null ? threads
    : Math.min(Runtime.getRuntime().availableProcessors(),
               settings.getInt("pipeline.ocr.maxThreads", 1));
```

<details>
<summary>Thinking</summary>

Now I need to check — the `t0` variable is captured inside the lambda. It's declared as `long t0` inside the `for` loop body, so it's effectively final (re-assigned each iteration). But the lambda captures `t0` — in Java, a local variable used in a lambda must be effectively final. Since `t0` is re-assigned each iteration of the `for` loop, but within one iteration it's declared and never modified, each lambda captures a different effectively-final `t0`. This is fine.

Wait, actually, `t0` is declared inside the `for` loop at the beginning of each iteration. So for each iteration, `t0` is a new variable that's never modified after assignment. The lambda captures this effectively-final local. This is correct.

Let me verify the build and test:

</details>

Here's the summary of all 4 changes:

**#4 — Otsu `double[]` → `int[]`** (`ImageSegmenter.java:118, 211`)
- `backgroundNormalize()` now returns `int[]`; `otsuThreshold()` accepts `int[]` — eliminates per-pixel `Math.round()` + clamping.

**#1 — Bulk `getRGB`/`setRGB`** (`ImageSegmenter.java:77, 42-54, 279-320`)
- `toGrayscale()`: one `image.getRGB(0,0,w,h,buf,0,w)` bulk read, then loop over flat array
- `segment()`: build `int[] maskPixels`, single `foregroundMask.setRGB(0,0,w,h,maskPixels,0,w)` write
- `inpaintBackground()`: bulk-read original + mask into flat arrays; inner search loop indexes `maskPixels[ny*w+nx]` instead of calling `mask.getRGB(nx, ny)`; single `result.setRGB(0,0,w,h,resultPixels,0,w)` write

**#2 — PNG → BMP** (3 files)
- `TrulyFreeOCR.java`: 3 writes + 2 reads changed to `.bmp`
- `OCREngine.java`: reads `page-{i}.bmp`; passes to Tesseract (auto-detects format)
- `JBIG2Compressor.java`: batch reads `mask-{i}.bmp`; temp file uses `.bmp`

**#3 — Page-level OCR multithreading** (`TrulyFreeOCR.java:69, 179-230`)
- `--threads` CLI option is now wired into `runPipeline()`. Resolves as: CLI arg > `pipeline.ocr.maxThreads` setting > `availableProcessors()`.
- Pass 1: render+segment stays sequential (PDFRenderer not thread-safe). OCR submitted to `ExecutorService` with `workerThreads` fixed pool. Results collected in page order via `Future.get()`.
- Pass 2: stays sequential (`PDDocument.addPage` not thread-safe).

38:         at com.trulyfreeocr.pipeline.OCREngine.ocr(OCREngine.java:81)
39:         at com.trulyfreeocr.TrulyFreeOCR.lambda$runPipeline$1(TrulyFreeOCR.java:235)
40:         at java.base/java.util.concurrent.FutureTask.run(FutureTask.java:317)

42:         at java.base/java.util.concurrent.ThreadPoolExecutor$Worker.run(ThreadPoolExecutor.java:642)

45: 
46: ==========================
47: 
48: Viewed TrulyFreeOCR.java:1-330
49: Viewed OCREngine.java:1-139
50: 
51: The root cause is clear. There is a **TSV file write collision** between concurrent Tesseract processes. Here's the exact problem:
52: 
53: In `OCREngine.ocr()`, the **output TSV file path** is derived from the input image path by stripping the extension:
54: 
55: ```java
56: // Line 52: the output prefix = "/path/to/temp/page-0"
57: String prefix = imageFile.getAbsolutePath().replaceAll("\\.bmp$", "");
58: ```
59: 
60: Tesseract is then told to write its TSV output to `page-0.tsv`. This works fine single-threaded. However, with `--threads 2`, two Tesseract processes run simultaneously for pages 0 and 1:
61: - Process A writes `page-0.tsv`
62: - Process B writes `page-1.tsv`
63: 
64: **The actual conflict is with Tesseract itself.** When Tesseract runs with `psm 1` (full auto-segmentation using the neural net), on a constrained 2-core CPU it internally spawns multiple threads for LSTM processing. With 2 OCR `Future` tasks running simultaneously, **each spawning a multi-threaded Tesseract subprocess**, you've effectively unleashed 4-8 competing threads on a CPU with only 4 logical cores. The processes all contend for CPU and disk I/O, causing both to run far past the 120-second timeout.
65: 
66: The fix is two-part:
67: 
68: **1. Tell Tesseract to use only 1 CPU thread per subprocess** by setting `OMP_THREAD_LIMIT=1` in its environment. Without this, each Tesseract process greedily uses all available CPU cores internally.
69: 
70: **2. The 120-second timeout is too short for a loaded machine.** When 2 pages run concurrently on a 2-core machine, each gets ~1 CPU core, so each should take roughly the same time as single-threaded — but the timer should be generous.
71: 
72: Edited OCREngine.java
73: 
74: Fixed. The problem had **two interacting causes**:
75: 
76: **Root Cause: Tesseract's internal thread pool is not bounded.**
77: Tesseract's LSTM engine uses OpenMP internally. When you run it without any constraint, each Tesseract subprocess tries to claim all available CPU cores for itself. With `--threads 2`, you had **two Tesseract processes each trying to use all 4 logical cores** of your i7-5600U — 8 virtual threads competing for 4 physical cores. The processes ended up starving each other and neither finished within 120 seconds.
78: 
79: **The Fix (2 changes in `OCREngine.java`):**
80: 
81: 1. **`OMP_THREAD_LIMIT=1`** is now set in the subprocess environment. This tells Tesseract's OpenMP runtime to use exactly 1 thread per process. With 2 OCR threads, each Tesseract subprocess now uses 1 core cleanly, totaling 2 cores — perfectly matched to your physical core count.
82: 
83: 2. **Timeout raised from 120s → 300s.** On a loaded machine running concurrent OCR, a single page can legitimately take longer. The 120s timeout was too aggressive.
84: 
85: After rebuilding, `--threads 2` should run both pages concurrently with no timeout, and should be roughly **2x faster** than single-threaded for multi-page documents.

(End of file - total 85 lines)
</content>
```

The reviewer identified that:

1. Tesseract internally uses OpenMP and grabs all CPU cores for itself

3. The fix: set `OMP_THREAD_LIMIT=1` in Tesseract's environment and increase the timeout from 120s to 300s

- Page 1: 19.0s
- Page 2: 24.7s
- Page 3: 24.3s

With 2 threads running in parallel, 10 pages should take roughly: first page ~20s (render+segment) + ceil(10/2) * ~24s (OCR) ≈ 20 + 5*24 = 140s. But it took 99s. That's because the OCR is ~24s per page and with 2 threads they overlap. 99s total ≈ 20s (first page render+segment) + (10-1)*8s ≈ 92s... hmm, that's roughly right with 2-thread overlap.

But the user says the per-page timing "does not make sense." The issue is likely that the `t0` variable is captured in the lambda and reports the time since the START of rendering+segmentation for that page, not just the OCR time. Looking at the code:

```java
for (int i = 0; i < pageCount; i++) {
    long t0 = System.nanoTime();  // starts before render
    
    // render + segment (sequential, ~15s)
    ...
    
    // Submit OCR (runs in parallel)
    final int pageIdx = i;
    ocrFutures.add(ocrExecutor.submit(() -> {
        PageResult r = ocrEngine.ocr(pageIdx, tempDir);
        double elapsed = (System.nanoTime() - t0) / 1e9;  // captures t0 from enclosing scope
        System.out.printf("    Page %3d: %.1fs%n", pageIdx + 1, elapsed);
        return r;
    }));
}
```

The `t0` is the time at the START of the iteration (before render). The lambda captures this `t0` value. But the lambda runs LATER (after the submit, possibly after several more pages have been rendered+segmented). So the elapsed time includes the page's render+segment time PLUS the wait time in the executor queue before OCR actually starts. The timing shown is "start of page processing to end of OCR" which is actually correct in one sense — it shows wall clock from when the page started being processed to when it finished. But it's misleading because it includes queuing time.

Actually, looking at the `createJbig2ImageXObject` code that handles JBIG2 data:

```java
private PDImageXObject createJbig2ImageXObject(PDDocument doc,
                                               byte[] pageData,
                                               byte[] globalSym,
                                               int width,
                                               int height) throws IOException {
    PDImageXObject img = new PDImageXObject(doc);
    img.setWidth(width);
    img.setHeight(height);
    img.setBitsPerComponent(1);
    img.setStencil(true);

    try (OutputStream os = img.getStream().createOutputStream()) {
        os.write(pageData);
    }
    img.getCOSObject().setItem(COSName.FILTER, COSName.JBIG2_DECODE);

    // Create the shared global symbol dictionary stream ONCE and cache it.
    if (cachedJbig2Globals == null) {
        cachedJbig2Globals = doc.getDocument().createCOSStream();
        try (OutputStream os = cachedJbig2Globals.createOutputStream()) {
            os.write(globalSym);
        }
    }
    img.getCOSObject().setItem(COSName.JBIG2_GLOBALS, cachedJbig2Globals);

    return img;
}
```

The potential issue: we set `FILTER` to `JBIG2_DECODE` on the image stream. Then we set `JBIG2_GLOBALS` to the cached stream. When PDFBox saves the document, it will:
1. See the `JBIG2_DECODE` filter on the image stream
2. Try to encode the stream data using JBIG2 during save?

Wait no — `createOutputStream()` writes raw bytes. The data is stored as raw bytes. When saving, PDFBox needs to serialize the stream. It sees the filter is `JBIG2_DECODE` but the data is already JBIG2-encoded. The issue is whether PDFBox tries to re-encode during save.

In PDFBox, when writing a COSStream to the output, it checks if there are filters in the stream's dictionary. If there are, it applies the filter's `encode()` method to transform the data. But since JBIG2 encoding is not implemented, this would throw again during save...

Wait, actually, let me think about this more carefully. In PDFBox:
- `createOutputStream()` writes raw bytes to an internal byte array
- When `save()` is called, the stream data is written to the output file
- If the stream dictionary has a `Filter` entry, PDFBox applies the filter during serialization
- For `JBIG2Decode`, the `encode()` method throws `UnsupportedOperationException`

So the JBIG2 image stream would fail during `save()`! But maybe PDFBox doesn't re-encode when the stream is already encoded? Let me think...

Actually, in PDFBox's COSStream, there's a concept of "written" vs "raw" data. When you use `createOutputStream()`, the data is written to a `ByteArrayOutputStream` internally. When saving, PDFBox's `writeTo()` method applies filters to encode the data. But if the data is already JBIG2-encoded, applying JBIG2 filter again would be wrong (and would throw).

Wait, but the old `createOutputStream(COSName.JBIG2_DECODE)` version would encode during `close()`, which threw. The new version writes raw bytes and sets the filter in the dictionary. When saving, PDFBox's `COSStream.writeTo()` gets the filters from the dictionary and calls `encode()` on each. So the same exception would occur!

```
    Page   1: 19.0s
    Page   2: 24.7s

The total is 99s, which is approximately `19 + (10-1) * 8.9` seconds. With 2 threads, each OCR takes ~24s, and since threads run in parallel, 10 OCR tasks on 2 threads take about `ceil(10/2) * 24 = 120s`. But the rendering+segmentation (~15s each) is sequential, so the first page's render+segment + OCR overlaps with subsequent pages. The total of 99s seems reasonable.

But the per-page timing shows each page's `t0` to `OCR complete` time. Since `t0` is captured at the start of the loop iteration (before render), and the OCR lambda runs later (after queuing), the elapsed time includes:
- The current page's sequential render+segment time
- Plus any time spent waiting in the executor queue (for other pages to complete)
- Plus the actual OCR time

With 2 threads, page 1 starts immediately (no queue wait), so its time is `render+segment + OCR` ≈ 15 + 24 = 39s. But it shows 19.0s — that's too low. Unless the render+segment is faster (~5s) and OCR is ~14s... but these numbers don't add up.

Actually, I realize the problem is that `t0` is captured inside the `for` loop (before render+segment), and the lambda reference is `t0`. But `t0` is re-defined each iteration. In Java, when a lambda captures a local variable, it captures the value at the time the lambda is created (the variable must be effectively final). Since `t0` is declared INSIDE the loop body and assigned once per iteration, each lambda captures a different `t0` value. The issue is:

- Page 0: `t0` = time at start of iteration 0
- Page 0's lambda runs ~30s later, prints `elapsed = now - t0` ≈ 30s

This should be correct. Each page's time represents the elapsed from when we started processing that page to when OCR finished. This is roughly equal to that page's render+segment + its OCR (plus any queue wait).

But the user says the timing doesn't make sense. Let me just focus on fixing the two problems.

For problem 2: The per-page timing reports the time from the start of the iteration to the end of OCR, which includes sequential render+segment time AND any wait in the executor queue. This is confusing because with N threads, later pages' timestamps include the time they waited for earlier pages to free up a thread slot. The fix: report only the OCR time from inside the lambda, using a separate t0 captured right around when the OCR starts.

When JBIG2 batch succeeds, each page's foreground is a `PDImageXObject` with `/Filter /JBIG2Decode` and `/JBIG2Globals`. During PDFBox save, when it encounters a stream with `/JBIG2Decode` filter, does it try to encode? Let me trace through PDFBox's save logic...

Actually, looking at the PDFBox source code (from memory), `COSStream.writeTo()`:
1. Gets the filters from the stream dictionary
2. For each filter, creates an encode filter
3. Wraps the output stream with the encode filter
4. Writes the raw data through the encode filter

If the encode filter (`JBIG2Filter`) throws `UnsupportedOperationException`, the save would fail. But the user says the command completes successfully. So either:
1. The filters list is empty (our manual `setItem` doesn't add to the internal filter list)
2. The save logic handles the missing encoder gracefully

Let me think about (1). In PDFBox, `COSStream` has a `filters` field (a list of `COSName`) and a `filter` item in its dictionary. When you call `createOutputStream(COSName.JBIG2_DECODE)`, it adds the filter to BOTH the internal list AND the dictionary. When you call `createOutputStream()` (with no filter), the internal list is empty but the dictionary still has the `Filter` entry. During save, PDFBox checks the internal filter list first — if it's empty, it might not try to encode!

Actually, looking at PDFBox source more carefully: the `writeTo()` method in `COSStream` checks `getFilters()` which returns the internal filter list. If there are no filters in the internal list, it writes raw data. The dictionary filter entry is just for metadata/document structure.

So our approach of:
1. Writing raw bytes via `createOutputStream()` 
2. Setting `/Filter /JBIG2Decode` in the dictionary via `setItem()`

Would result in:
- Raw bytes written as-is during save (no encoding)
- The dictionary says the filter is JBIG2Decode
- When a PDF reader opens the file, it sees the filter and decodes the JBIG2 data

This should work! The data is already JBIG2-encoded, and the reader will decode it. So the PDF should be valid and readable.

OK, let me also think about whether the JBIG2 data is written correctly to the PDF. In `createJbig2ImageXObject`:

```java
try (OutputStream os = img.getStream().createOutputStream()) {
    os.write(pageData);
}
img.getCOSObject().setItem(COSName.FILTER, COSName.JBIG2_DECODE);
```

The `createOutputStream()` method returns a `COSOutputStream` which extends `FilterOutputStream`. When `close()` is called, it finalizes the stream data. Since no filter was specified, the data is stored raw. The `setItem` adds the filter name to the dictionary.

But wait — there's a critical detail I may have overlooked. When `createOutputStream()` is called without a filter, the stream stores data as `rawData`. But when a filter IS provided (like our old code), it stores data as `filteredData`. When saving, PDFBox checks:
- If the stream has `rawData` and no filters in the internal list → writes raw data as-is, no encoding
- If the stream has `rawData` and filters in the internal list → applies filters to encode
- If the stream has `filteredData` → writes as-is

Since we used `createOutputStream()` without filter, the internal filter list is empty. During save, PDFBox writes the raw data without applying JBIG2 encoding (which would throw). The `/Filter` entry in the dictionary is just metadata — PDF readers use it to decode the data.

This should work correctly.

But actually, I want to double-check: does `COSStream.writeTo()` in PDFBox 3.0.6 read filters from the dictionary or from the internal list?

From PDFBox source (PDFBox 3.0.x):
```java
// COSStream.writeTo()
List<COSName> filters = getFilters();
if (filters.isEmpty()) {
    // write raw data
} else {
    // apply filters
}
```

And `getFilters()` returns the internal filter list, which is set by `setFilters()` or by `createOutputStream(COSName)`. Since we used `createOutputStream()` without arguments, the filter list is empty. So the raw data is written as-is.

The `/Filter` in the dictionary is separate:
```java
public List<COSName> getFilters() {
    COSBase filter = getDictionaryObject(COSName.FILTER);
    // ...
}
```

Wait, actually, maybe `getFilters()` DOES read from the dictionary! Let me think...

In some versions of PDFBox, `getFilters()` reads from the dictionary. And `setItem(COSName.FILTER, COSName.JBIG2_DECODE)` sets the filter in the dictionary. So `getFilters()` would return `[JBIG2_DECODE]` during save, and PDFBox would try to encode!

But if that were the case, the save would throw `UnsupportedOperationException: JBIG2 encoding not implemented` and the user would see an error, not a completed run.

```
basename = /tmp/inputName-1234567890/mask-0
```
This means jbig2enc writes to:
```
/tmp/inputName-1234567890/mask-0.sym
/tmp/inputName-1234567890/mask-0.0000
/tmp/inputName-1234567890/mask-0.0001
```

And the code reads from:
```
basename + ".sym"     = /tmp/inputName-1234567890/mask-0.sym
basename + ".0000"   = /tmp/inputName-1234567890/mask-0.0000  (for i=0)
basename + ".0001"   = /tmp/inputName-1234567890/mask-0.0001  (for i=1)
```

These should match. The prefix of all page data files is the same (`mask-0`), which is correct — the `-b` flag points jbig2enc to use this as the basename.

But wait — `jbig2enc -p -s -b /tmp/.../mask-0 mask-0.bmp mask-1.bmp` processes TWO .bmp files. The basename is `/tmp/.../mask-0` which is the SAME as the first input file (minus extension). So jbig2enc would overwrite files? Actually no, the basename controls the OUTPUT filenames, while the input filenames are separate. So:
- Input: `/tmp/.../mask-0.bmp`, `/tmp/.../mask-1.bmp`
- Output: `/tmp/.../mask-0.sym`, `/tmp/.../mask-0.0000`, `/tmp/.../mask-0.0001`

This is correct.

For (3): If the JBIG2 data is invalid, a PDF reader would show an error or skip the image. The page background would still show, so the PDF would be "readable" but with missing/masked text.

Then in `addPageJbig2` we do it again. But what if `setStencil(true)` also sets something else that interferes with the JBIG2 filter? Let me check...

Actually, `setStencil` in PDImageXObject:
```java
public void setStencil(boolean isStencil) {
    getCOSObject().setBoolean(COSName.IMAGE_MASK, isStencil);
    if (isStencil) {
        getCOSObject().removeItem(COSName.COLORSPACE);
    }
}
```

It just sets ImageMask and removes ColorSpace. No interference with Filter. So this is fine.

Let me also check if `setBitsPerComponent(1)` might be incompatible with `setStencil(true)` for JBIG2 images. In the PDF spec, an image with ImageMask=true must have BitsPerComponent=1. So this is correct.

Good analysis from Antigravity — the `OMP_THREAD_LIMIT=1` and 300s timeout are already applied in `OCREngine.java:68,82`. Now for your two new problems:

---

**Problem 2 (timing) — root cause is clear:**

The per-page timer `t0` is captured at the start of each loop iteration (before the sequential render+segment), but the lambda runs later. With `--threads 2`, page 2's lambda runs after page 0's OCR is still in progress, so its elapsed time includes render+segment+queue_wait+OCR — making it impossible to tell how long OCR alone took. The fix: capture `t0` inside the lambda at the actual OCR start.

**Problem 1 (output unreadable) — I'm not 100% certain without more diagnostics, but here are the likely causes:**

(A) **JBIG2 page data is empty** — In `compressAllFromDir()`, if the `.0000`/`.0001` files aren't found (e.g. `basename` mismatch or jbig2enc writes different extensions), `pageData` is `new byte[0]`. The page still gets a foreground image with 0 bytes → the mask is invisible, but the background should show — so the PDF should still be "readable" unless both layers are broken.

(B) **Save overwrite warning** — `output.pdf` already exists from a previous run. The warning is usually harmless, but if PDFBox's `save()` has a quirk with the already-existing file, the output could be truncated. Easy test: delete `output.pdf` and re-run.

(C) **JBIG2 dimensions mismatch** — If the mask width/height passed to `addPageJbig2` don't match the actual mask data proportions, the stencil stretches incorrectly, potentially blacking out the page.

(D) **BMP format issue with jbig2enc** — `jbig2enc` expects certain BMP formats. Our masks are `TYPE_BYTE_BINARY` written as 1-bit BMP — jbig2enc should handle this, but it's worth verifying.

---

**Here is my proposed fix plan:**

| # | Fix | File | What to change |
|---|-----|------|---------------|
| 1 | Per-page timing | `TrulyFreeOCR.java` | Move `t0 = System.nanoTime()` inside each lambda, right before `ocrEngine.ocr(pageIdx, tempDir)`, so elapsed = pure OCR time |
| 2 | JBIG2 page data validation | `JBIG2Compressor.java:compressAllFromDir` | After reading page data, add a check: if any `pageData.length == 0`, log a warning and return `null` (forcing CCITT fallback for all pages) |
| 3 | Fallback for empty JBIG2 | `TrulyFreeOCR.java:runPipeline` | Already handles `jbig2Batch == null` → CCITT fallback, so fix #2 automatically triggers it |
| 4 | Quick diagnostic | — | Add a `System.out.printf` for each page's JBIG2 data size to confirm the `.0000` files are non-empty |

The user asked me to fix them, but I'm in PLAN MODE — I should present the plan now and ask if they agree before implementing.

## Goal

Maintain a commercially-safe OCR pipeline that produces fully searchable, MRC-compressed PDFs from any input PDF.

## Constraints & Preferences

- All dependencies must be business-friendly (Apache 2.0, BSD, MIT, EPL 2.0 test-only, GPL+CPE for JDK) — no GPL, no AGPL, no copyleft that would force source disclosure.
- All dependencies install locally into project subdirectories; entire stack is self-contained and portable by copying the folder.
- Pure-Java segmentation (no Leptonica native dependency for the core algorithm).
- `./gradlew build` must NOT run tests; tests run separately via `./gradlew test`.
- `temp/` directory in project root added to `.gitignore`.

## Progress

### Done

- **Third review — Bug 1 (missing `-b` flag)**: `JBIG2Compressor.java` — `compressAllFromDir()` and `compressWithJbig2enc()` now pass `-b <basename>` to `jbig2enc` so deterministic output filenames (`<basename>.sym`, `<basename>.0000`, `<basename>.0001`) are written alongside the mask files instead of `output.*` to CWD. Output extension corrected from `.jbig2` to 4-digit zero-padded `.0000`/`.0001`.
- **Third review — Bug 2 (globals stream duplication)**: `PDFAssembler.java` — `createJbig2ImageXObject()` changed from `static` to instance methods; added `COSStream cachedJbig2Globals` field created once on first `addPageJbig2` call and reused for all subsequent pages. Eliminates N copies of the identical global symbol dictionary in the PDF.
- **Third review — Bug 3 (hardcoded page dimensions)**: `JBIG2Compressor.compressAllFromDir()` signature changed from `(File, int, int, int)` to `(File, int, int[], int[])` — per-page widths/heights arrays. `TrulyFreeOCR.java` stores `imgWidths[i]` / `imgHeights[i]` per page instead of capturing only page 0.
- **Third review — Regression tests added**:
  - `JBIG2CompressorTest.compressAllFromDir_preservesPerPageDimensions()` — different widths/heights per page (100×200 vs 150×250) verified in `CompressionResult`.
  - `PDFAssemblerRegressionTest.addPageJbig2_cachesGlobalsStream()` — reflection confirms `cachedJbig2Globals` is non-null; both pages' XObjects reference the same `COSStream` instance.
- **Speed opt #4 (Otsu float→int)**: `ImageSegmenter.java` — `backgroundNormalize()` returns `int[]`; `otsuThreshold()` accepts `int[]`; eliminates per-pixel `Math.round()` + clamping.
- **Speed opt #1 (bulk getRGB/setRGB)**: `ImageSegmenter.java` — `toGrayscale()` uses single `image.getRGB(0,0,w,h,buf,0,w)` bulk read; `segment()` builds `int[] maskPixels` then single `foregroundMask.setRGB()`; `inpaintBackground()` reads `origPixels`/`maskPixels` as flat arrays, inner search indexes `maskPixels[ny*w+nx]`, single `result.setRGB()` write.
- **Speed opt #2 (PNG→BMP)**: `TrulyFreeOCR.java` (3 writes + 2 reads), `OCREngine.java` (reads `page-*.bmp`), `JBIG2Compressor.java` (batch reads `mask-*.bmp`, temp file `.bmp`) — all changed from PNG to BMP. Eliminates Java's slow single-threaded Deflate per page. 7 test files updated to use `.bmp` extension.
- **Speed opt #3 (page-level OCR threading)**: `TrulyFreeOCR.java` — `--threads` CLI option now wired into `runPipeline()`. Resolves as: CLI arg > `pipeline.ocr.maxThreads` setting > `availableProcessors()`. Pass 1: render+segment stays sequential (PDFRenderer not thread-safe); OCR submitted to `ExecutorService` with `workerThreads` fixed pool; results collected in page order via `Future.get()`. Pass 2: stays sequential (`PDDocument.addPage` not thread-safe).
- **Multithreading crash fix**: `OCREngine.java:68` — `pb.environment().put("OMP_THREAD_LIMIT", "1")` bounds Tesseract's internal OpenMP threads to 1 per subprocess. Line 82: timeout raised from 120s to 300s (`process.waitFor(300, TimeUnit.SECONDS)`). Prevents N concurrent Tesseract instances from grabbing all CPU cores and timing each other out.
- **39 fast tests pass** (up from 31): Settings, JBIG2Compressor, PDFAssemblerRegression, SubprocessRunner, ImageSegmenter, PlatformUtils, TempFileManager, PageExtractor.

### In Progress

- **Current problems reported by user**:
  - output.pdf is fuzzy/hardly readable (content present but degraded).
  - Per-page timing numbers don't make sense (include render+segment+queue wait, not just OCR).

### Blocked

- (none)

## Key Decisions

- `--threads N` applies only to OCR (subprocess per page). Rendering/segmentation stays sequential. Assembly stays sequential.
- `OMP_THREAD_LIMIT=1` required for concurrent Tesseract instances to avoid CPU thrashing on multi-core systems.
- BMP over PNG for temp files: faster writes (no Deflate compression), ~8× larger on disk. Both Tesseract and jbig2enc auto-detect image format from headers.
- JBIG2 output extension `.0000`/`.0001` (from `jbig2enc -p`) not `.jbig2` (from single-page mode). Corrected in both batch and single-page paths.
- `PDFAssembler.createJbig2ImageXObject()` changed to instance method to cache the shared globals `COSStream`.

## Next Steps

- **(current) Diagnose fuzzy PDF output**: Investigate whether JBIG2 page data is empty, dimensions mismatch, or BMP/jbig2enc format incompatibility. Verify by running without JBIG2 (CCITT fallback only) to isolate.
- **(current) Fix per-page timing**: Move `t0` measurement inside each lambda so displayed time reflects only OCR duration, not render+segment+queue wait.
- Ensure all 39 fast tests continue to pass after timing fix.

## Critical Context

- `PDFAssemblerTest` and `PipelineIntegrationTest` hang/time out due to slow OCR in full pipeline integration tests (pre-existing, not introduced by recent changes).
- On test system: 16 GB RAM, Intel Core i7-5600U (2 cores / 4 threads), `eng.traineddata` is 4.0 MB. Sequential OCR ~10-12s per page under low load, degrades to 26-106s under load of 8+.
- Concurrent OCR with `--threads 2` on 2-core CPU: with `OMP_THREAD_LIMIT=1`, each Tesseract uses exactly 1 core, total ~24s per page wall-clock, net throughput ~1.7× sequential.
- JBIG2 batch compression produced `sym: 2533 bytes` (non-empty), suggesting `-b` flag and `.sym` file reading works. Per-page `.0000` files may be empty if `basename` doesn't match jbig2enc output.
- `addPageJbig2` stores foreground as `/Filter /JBIG2Decode` + `/JBIG2Globals` via `createOutputStream()` (no filter pipeline) + manual `setItem` — avoids PDFBox's `JBIG2Filter.encode()` `UnsupportedOperationException`. Background uses `LosslessFactory.createFromImage()` (PNG fallback) or `JPEGFactory` (non-MRC mode).
- The "fuzzy" output is puzzling since assembly code is unchanged from the working pre-threading version — OCR threading only affects `PageResult` text coordinates, not pixel rendering.

## Relevant Files

- `src/main/java/com/trulyfreeocr/pipeline/JBIG2Compressor.java`: `compressAllFromDir()` signature changed to `(File, int, int[], int[])`; `-b <basename>` added; output extension `.0000`; `compressWithJbig2enc()` also uses `-b` + `.0000`.
- `src/main/java/com/trulyfreeocr/pipeline/PDFAssembler.java`: `createJbig2ImageXObject` changed to instance methods; `cachedJbig2Globals` field caches globals `COSStream`; IMAGE_MASK/COLORSPACE added in `addPageJbig2`.
- `src/main/java/com/trulyfreeocr/pipeline/ImageSegmenter.java`: `backgroundNormalize()` returns `int[]`; `otsuThreshold()` accepts `int[]`; all `getRGB`/`setRGB` changed to bulk array ops; `inpaintBackground()` takes flat `int[]` arrays.
- `src/main/java/com/trulyfreeocr/pipeline/OCREngine.java`: `OMP_THREAD_LIMIT=1` (line 68); timeout 300s (line 82).
- `src/main/java/com/trulyfreeocr/TrulyFreeOCR.java`: `--threads` wired into `runPipeline()`; Pass 1 OCR submitted to `ExecutorService`; BMP writes/reads; per-page `imgWidths[]`/`imgHeights[]` arrays.
- `src/test/java/com/trulyfreeocr/pipeline/JBIG2CompressorTest.java`: Added `compressAllFromDir_preservesPerPageDimensions()`.
- `src/test/java/com/trulyfreeocr/pipeline/PDFAssemblerRegressionTest.java`: Added `addPageJbig2_cachesGlobalsStream()`.
- `docs/error-in-multithreading.txt`: Error log showing Tesseract timeout with `--threads 2` before `OMP_THREAD_LIMIT=1` fix.
