<summary>Thinking</summary>

Now I see the timing issue clearly. On line 191, `t0` is captured BEFORE the rendering and segmentation. Then on line 225, the lambda captures `t0` and uses it to measure OCR time. But by the time the lambda runs, `t0` is already hundreds of ms old (the capture happens in the loop body, but the lambda captures the variable, not the value... wait, actually `t0` is a `long` (primitive), so Java captures its value when the lambda is created. But the lambda is created on line 223, at which point `t0` is already several seconds old (render + segment + background save + mask save + bg save have already completed). So the "OCR time" printed includes all the render+segment time.

The fix would be to measure `t0` inside the lambda, right at the start of the OCR call.

Now let me focus on what the user actually asked: they stopped me because they want me to diagnose the fuzzy output BEFORE fixing the timing issue. Let me think about what could cause "very fuzzy, hardly readable" output.

Actually, looking at the user's message more carefully: "The pages seem to have some content, but very fuzzy, hardly readable."

Wait — the user also said "I have selected the content" and pasted it. The selection they showed was `output.pdf.txt` which contains English text like "TrulyFreeOCR" — wait, no, that was the text they selected from the about statement. Let me re-read.

Actually, `output.pdf.txt` was created by selecting content from the PDF and pasting it. The user is saying they could select and copy text, but it doesn't look like what the text should be. Wait, they said they shared it so I can see what was captured. The text seems plausible (technical content about PDF structure).

The key question is: is the "fuzzy" appearance:
1. A new problem introduced by the recent changes (threading, BMP), or
2. Something that was always there (the user never had a successful run before)?

Given that the user says "pages seem to have some content but very fuzzy", I think they had an earlier version that produced crisper output, and the current version is worse.

Let me think about what changed that could affect visual quality:
1. PNG → BMP: converting background and mask to BMP instead of PNG. BMP is lossless, so this shouldn't affect quality.
2. Threading: shouldn't affect quality, only OCR
3. JBIG2 batch compression: was already tested and worked
4. Bug fixes (globs stream dedup, -b flag, per-page dimensions): these should help, not hurt

Wait — could the issue be with the background image's TYPE after BMP round-trip? When we write a `TYPE_BYTE_GRAY` image as BMP and read it back, does `ImageIO.read()` return a `TYPE_BYTE_GRAY` image? Let me think...

Java's BMP ImageWriter for a `TYPE_BYTE_GRAY` image will write an 8-bit grayscale BMP. When read back, the ImageIO BMP reader typically creates a `TYPE_BYTE_INDEXED` image (indexed with a grayscale palette) for 8-bit BMPs, not a `TYPE_BYTE_GRAY`. This could potentially cause issues with:
1. `PDFBox`'s `LosslessFactory.createFromImage()` — does it handle `TYPE_BYTE_INDEXED` correctly? Actually PDFBox does support `TYPE_BYTE_INDEXED` and `TYPE_BYTE_GRAY` in LosslessFactory. Both should be encoded as PNG Deflate streams correctly.

But what about `JPEGFactory`? In the non-MRC path, the background goes through `JPEGFactory.createFromImage(output, background, 0.85f)`. If the background is `TYPE_BYTE_INDEXED` instead of `TYPE_BYTE_GRAY`, JPEGFactory might handle it differently — converting to RGB (24-bit) before JPEG encoding. This would look fine, just larger.

Actually, the JBIG2 path uses `LosslessFactory.createFromImage()` exclusively (not JPEGFactory), so TYPE differences shouldn't matter much.

Hmm wait — actually let me reconsider. Could the issue be the 0.85f JPEG quality? In the non-MRC path:
```java
bgXObject = JPEGFactory.createFromImage(output, background, 0.85f);
```

0.85 JPEG quality is decent but at high DPI (300), JPEG artifacts in smooth areas might be visible. But in MRC mode, this code path isn't used (the foreground mask is non-null).

Wait, I should re-read `PDFAssembler.addPage()` more carefully.

Let me look at the assembly code again.

```
20: import org.apache.pdfbox.pdmodel.font.PDType0Font;
21: import org.apache.pdfbox.pdmodel.font.PDType1Font;
22: import org.apache.pdfbox.pdmodel.font.Standard14Fonts;
23: import org.apache.pdfbox.pdmodel.graphics.color.PDOutputIntent;
24: import org.apache.pdfbox.pdmodel.graphics.image.CCITTFactory;
25: import org.apache.pdfbox.pdmodel.graphics.image.JPEGFactory;
26: import org.apache.pdfbox.pdmodel.graphics.image.LosslessFactory;
27: import org.apache.pdfbox.pdmodel.graphics.image.PDImageXObject;
28: import org.apache.pdfbox.pdmodel.graphics.state.RenderingMode;
29: import org.apache.pdfbox.util.Matrix;
30: 
31: import com.trulyfreeocr.model.PageResult;
32: import com.trulyfreeocr.model.TextBlock;
33: import com.trulyfreeocr.util.Settings;
34: 
35: /**
36:  * Re-assembles a searchable PDF from per-page inputs:
37:  *   - Source PDF (copied for page dimensions / media boxes)
38:  *   - Cleaned background images
39:  *   - Binary foreground masks (CCITT G4 compressed stencil overlay)
40:  *   - OCR results (invisible text layer)
41:  *
42:  * MRC-like layout (per page):
43:  *   1. Background layer: cleaned page image (Lossless PNG for now).
44:  *   2. Foreground mask: CCITT G4 compressed binary mask, drawn as a
45:  *      PDF ImageMask stencil in black — reveals only the text pixels
46:  *      on top of the background.
47:  *   3. Text layer: invisible OCR text (RenderingMode.NEITHER = Tr 3),
48:  *      selectable and searchable but not visible.
49:  *
50:  * Rendering mode NEITHER makes text invisible on screen/print while
51:  * keeping it selectable and searchable.
52:  *
53:  * Coordinate transformation:
54:  *   Page images at 300 DPI → PDF user space at 72 DPI.
55:  *   scaleX = pageWidth_pts  / imageWidth_px
56:  *   scaleY = pageHeight_pts / imageHeight_px
57:  *   Y is flipped: image origin is top-left, PDF origin is bottom-left.
58:  */
59: public class PDFAssembler {
60: 
61:     private final PDType1Font font;
62:     private final float minFontSize;
63:     private final MetadataPreserver preserver = new MetadataPreserver();
64:     private File pdfaFontFile;
65:     private JBIG2Compressor compressor;
66:     private COSStream cachedJbig2Globals;
67: 
68:     public PDFAssembler() {
69:         this("HELVETICA", 1f);
70:     }
71: 
72:     public PDFAssembler(String fontName, float minFontSize) {
73:         Standard14Fonts.FontName resolved;
74:         try {
75:             resolved = Standard14Fonts.FontName.valueOf(fontName);
76:         } catch (IllegalArgumentException e) {
77:             resolved = Standard14Fonts.FontName.HELVETICA;
78:         }
79:         this.font = new PDType1Font(resolved);
80:         this.minFontSize = minFontSize;
81:     }
82: 
83:     public void setPdfaFont(File fontFile) {
84:         this.pdfaFontFile = fontFile;
85:     }
86: 
87:     public void setCompressor(JBIG2Compressor compressor) {
88:         this.compressor = compressor;
89:     }
90: 
91:     /**
92:      * Builds a searchable PDF with MRC-like foreground mask overlay.
93:      *
94:      * When foregroundMasks is provided, each mask is CCITT G4 compressed
95:      * and drawn as a PDF ImageMask stencil in black on top of the background.
96:      * This reproduces the original text pixels at full sharpness, while
97:      * the background layer carries the cleaned (de-speckled) page image.
98:      *
99:      * @param sourcePdf       Original PDF (for per-page media boxes).
100:      * @param backgrounds     Per-page cleaned background images.
101:      * @param foregroundMasks Per-page binary foreground masks (TYPE_BYTE_BINARY,
102:      *                        black=text, white=background), or null to skip.
103:      * @param ocrResults      Per-page OCR results.
104:      * @param usePdfa         Enable PDF/A-2b output with XMP metadata.
105:      * @return A new PDDocument with foreground mask overlay and searchable text.
106:      */
107:     public PDDocument assemble(File sourcePdf,
108:                                List<BufferedImage> backgrounds,
109:                                List<BufferedImage> foregroundMasks,
110:                                List<PageResult> ocrResults,
111:                                boolean usePdfa) throws IOException {
112:         PDDocument output = new PDDocument();
113:         List<PDPage> outPages = new java.util.ArrayList<>();
114: 
115:         try (PDDocument source = Loader.loadPDF(sourcePdf)) {
116:             int pageCount = source.getNumberOfPages();
117:             for (int i = 0; i < pageCount; i++) {
118:                 BufferedImage bg = backgrounds.get(i);
119:                 BufferedImage fg = foregroundMasks != null ? foregroundMasks.get(i) : null;
120:                 PDPage page = addPage(output, source, i, bg, fg, ocrResults.get(i));
121:                 outPages.add(page);
122:             }
123:             finishAssembly(output, source, outPages, usePdfa);
124:         }
125: 
126:         return output;
127:     }
128: 
129:     /**
130:      * Renders one page into the output document.  Call repeatedly for
131:      * each page of the source, then call {@link #finishAssembly}.
132:      *
133:      * @param output        The output PDDocument being built.
134:      * @param source        The source PDDocument (already loaded).
135:      * @param pageIndex     0-based page index in source.
136:      * @param background    Cleaned background image for this page.
137:      * @param foregroundMask Binary mask or null to skip the stencil layer.
138:      * @param ocr           OCR result for this page.
139:      * @return The newly created PDPage added to output.
140:      */
141:     public PDPage addPage(PDDocument output, PDDocument source, int pageIndex,
142:                           BufferedImage background, BufferedImage foregroundMask,
143:                           PageResult ocr) throws IOException {
144:         PDPage sourcePage = source.getPage(pageIndex);
145:         PDRectangle mediaBox = sourcePage.getMediaBox();
146:         float pageW = mediaBox.getWidth();
147:         float pageH = mediaBox.getHeight();
148: 
149:         PDPage outPage = new PDPage(mediaBox);
150:         output.addPage(outPage);
151: 
152:         try (PDPageContentStream cs = new PDPageContentStream(output, outPage)) {
153:             // Layer 1: background image
154:             PDImageXObject bgXObject;
155:             if (foregroundMask == null) {
156:                 bgXObject = JPEGFactory.createFromImage(output, background, 0.85f);
157:             } else {
158:                 bgXObject = LosslessFactory.createFromImage(output, background);
159:             }
160:             cs.drawImage(bgXObject, 0, 0, pageW, pageH);
161: 
162:             // Layer 2: foreground mask as CCITT G4 stencil overlay (JBIG2 when available)
163:             if (foregroundMask != null) {
164:                 PDImageXObject fgImage;
165:                 if (compressor != null) {
166:                     JBIG2Compressor.CompressionResult result = compressor.compress(foregroundMask);
167:                     if (result.isJbig2()) {
168:                         fgImage = createJbig2ImageXObject(output, result);
169:                     } else {
170:                         fgImage = CCITTFactory.createFromImage(output, foregroundMask);
171:                     }
172:                 } else {
173:                     fgImage = CCITTFactory.createFromImage(output, foregroundMask);
174:                 }
175:                 fgImage.getCOSObject().setBoolean(COSName.IMAGE_MASK, true);
176:                 fgImage.getCOSObject().removeItem(COSName.COLORSPACE);
177:                 cs.setNonStrokingColor(0f, 0f, 0f);
178:                 cs.drawImage(fgImage, 0, 0, pageW, pageH);
179:             }
180: 
181:             // Layer 3: invisible OCR text
182:             float scaleX = pageW / ocr.getWidth();
183:             float scaleY = pageH / ocr.getHeight();
184:             PDFont pageFont = (pdfaFontFile != null && pdfaFontFile.exists())
185:                     ? PDType0Font.load(output, pdfaFontFile)
186:                     : font;
187:             cs.beginText();
188:             cs.setFont(pageFont, minFontSize);
189:             cs.setRenderingMode(RenderingMode.NEITHER);
190: 
191:             for (TextBlock tb : ocr.getTextBlocks()) {
192:                 float x = tb.getBbox().x * scaleX;
193:                 float y = pageH - (tb.getBbox().y + tb.getBbox().height) * scaleY;
194:                 float fontSize = Math.max(tb.getBbox().height * scaleY, minFontSize);
195:                 cs.setFont(pageFont, fontSize);
196:                 cs.setTextMatrix(Matrix.getTranslateInstance(x, y));
197:                 cs.showText(tb.getWord());
198:             }
199:             cs.endText();
200:         }
201: 
202:         return outPage;
203:     }
204: 
205:     /**
206:      * Copies metadata from the source and optionally adds PDF/A-2b info.
207:      * Must be called after all {@link #addPage} calls are complete.
208:      */
209:     public void finishAssembly(PDDocument output, PDDocument source,
210:                                List<PDPage> outPages, boolean usePdfa) throws IOException {
211:         preserver.preserve(source, output, outPages);
212:         if (usePdfa) {
213:             addPdfaMetadata(output);
214:         }
215:     }
216: 
217:     private PDImageXObject createJbig2ImageXObject(PDDocument doc, JBIG2Compressor.CompressionResult result) throws IOException {
218:         PDImageXObject img = new PDImageXObject(doc);
219:         img.setWidth(result.getWidth());
220:         img.setHeight(result.getHeight());
221:         img.setBitsPerComponent(1);
222:         img.setStencil(true);
223:         try (OutputStream os = img.getStream().createOutputStream()) {
224:             os.write(result.getData());
225:         }
226:         img.getCOSObject().setItem(COSName.FILTER, COSName.JBIG2_DECODE);
227:         return img;
228:     }
229: 
230:     private PDImageXObject createJbig2ImageXObject(PDDocument doc,
231:                                                    byte[] pageData,
232:                                                    byte[] globalSym,
233:                                                    int width,
234:                                                    int height) throws IOException {
235:         PDImageXObject img = new PDImageXObject(doc);
236:         img.setWidth(width);
237:         img.setHeight(height);
238:         img.setBitsPerComponent(1);
239:         img.setStencil(true);
240: 
241:         // Write per-page JBIG2 data (no filter pipeline — raw bytes are already compressed)
242:         try (OutputStream os = img.getStream().createOutputStream()) {
243:             os.write(pageData);
244:         }
245:         img.getCOSObject().setItem(COSName.FILTER, COSName.JBIG2_DECODE);
246: 
247:         // Create the shared global symbol dictionary stream ONCE and cache it.
248:         // Reusing the same COSStream reference for every PDImageXObject avoids
249:         // embedding N copies of the identical global dictionary in the PDF.
250:         if (cachedJbig2Globals == null) {
251:             cachedJbig2Globals = doc.getDocument().createCOSStream();
252:             try (OutputStream os = cachedJbig2Globals.createOutputStream()) {
253:                 os.write(globalSym);
254:             }
255:         }
256:         img.getCOSObject().setItem(COSName.JBIG2_GLOBALS, cachedJbig2Globals);
257: 
258:         return img;
259:     }
260: 
261:     /**
262:      * Adds one page to the output document using pre-compressed JBIG2
263:      * foreground data with a shared global symbol dictionary.
264:      */
265:     public PDPage addPageJbig2(PDDocument output, PDDocument source, int pageIndex,
266:                                BufferedImage background,
267:                                byte[] jbig2PageData, byte[] jbig2GlobalSym,
268:                                int fgWidth, int fgHeight,
269:                                PageResult ocr) throws IOException {
270:         PDPage sourcePage = source.getPage(pageIndex);
271:         PDRectangle mediaBox = sourcePage.getMediaBox();
272:         float pageW = mediaBox.getWidth();
273:         float pageH = mediaBox.getHeight();
274: 
275:         PDPage outPage = new PDPage(mediaBox);
276:         output.addPage(outPage);
277: 
278:         try (PDPageContentStream cs = new PDPageContentStream(output, outPage)) {
279:             // Layer 1: background image
280:             PDImageXObject bgXObject = LosslessFactory.createFromImage(output, background);
281:             cs.drawImage(bgXObject, 0, 0, pageW, pageH);
282: 
283:             // Layer 2: JBIG2 foreground mask with shared globals
284:             if (jbig2PageData != null && jbig2GlobalSym != null) {
285:                 PDImageXObject fgImage = createJbig2ImageXObject(output,
286:                     jbig2PageData, jbig2GlobalSym, fgWidth, fgHeight);
287:                 fgImage.getCOSObject().setBoolean(COSName.IMAGE_MASK, true);
288:                 fgImage.getCOSObject().removeItem(COSName.COLORSPACE);
289:                 cs.setNonStrokingColor(0f, 0f, 0f);
290:                 cs.drawImage(fgImage, 0, 0, pageW, pageH);
291:             }
292: 
293:             // Layer 3: invisible OCR text
294:             float scaleX = pageW / ocr.getWidth();
295:             float scaleY = pageH / ocr.getHeight();
296:             PDFont pageFont = (pdfaFontFile != null && pdfaFontFile.exists())
297:                     ? PDType0Font.load(output, pdfaFontFile)
298:                     : font;
299:             cs.beginText();
300:             cs.setFont(pageFont, minFontSize);
301:             cs.setRenderingMode(RenderingMode.NEITHER);
302: 
303:             for (TextBlock tb : ocr.getTextBlocks()) {
304:                 float x = tb.getBbox().x * scaleX;
305:                 float y = pageH - (tb.getBbox().y + tb.getBbox().height) * scaleY;
306:                 float fontSize = Math.max(tb.getBbox().height * scaleY, minFontSize);
307:                 cs.setFont(pageFont, fontSize);
308:                 cs.setTextMatrix(Matrix.getTranslateInstance(x, y));
309:                 cs.showText(tb.getWord());
310:             }
311:             cs.endText();
312:         }
313: 
314:         return outPage;
315:     }
316: 
317:     private void addPdfaMetadata(PDDocument doc) throws IOException {
318:         PDDocumentCatalog catalog = doc.getDocumentCatalog();
319: 
320:         // PDF/A-2b XMP metadata
321:         String xmp = """
322:                 <?xpacket begin="\\uFEFF" id="W5M0MpCehiHzreSzNTczkc9d"?>
323:                 <x:xmpmeta xmlns:x="adobe:ns:meta/">
324:                   <rdf:RDF xmlns:rdf="http://www.w3.org/1999/02/22-rdf-syntax-ns#">
325:                     <rdf:Description rdf:about=""
326:                       xmlns:pdfaid="http://www.aiim.org/pdfa/ns/id/">
327:                       <pdfaid:part>2</pdfaid:part>
328:                       <pdfaid:conformance>B</pdfaid:conformance>
329:                     </rdf:Description>
330:                     <rdf:Description rdf:about=""
331:                       xmlns:dc="http://purl.org/dc/elements/1.1/">
332:                       <dc:format>application/pdf</dc:format>
333:                     </rdf:Description>
334:                   </rdf:RDF>
335:                 </x:xmpmeta>
336:                 <?xpacket end="w"?>""".stripIndent();
337: 
338:         org.apache.pdfbox.pdmodel.common.PDMetadata metadata =
339:                 new org.apache.pdfbox.pdmodel.common.PDMetadata(doc);
340:         metadata.importXMPMetadata(xmp.getBytes(StandardCharsets.UTF_8));
341:         catalog.setMetadata(metadata);
342: 
343:         // sRGB output intent
344:         try (InputStream srgbStream = getClass().getResourceAsStream("/sRGB Color Space Profile.icm")) {
345:             if (srgbStream != null) {
346:                 PDOutputIntent intent = new PDOutputIntent(doc, srgbStream);
347:                 intent.setInfo("sRGB IEC61966-2.1");
348:                 intent.setOutputCondition("sRGB IEC61966-2.1");
349:                 intent.setOutputConditionIdentifier("sRGB IEC61966-2.1");
350:                 intent.setRegistryName("http://www.color.org");
351:                 catalog.addOutputIntent(intent);
352:             }
353:         }
354:     }
355: 
356: }

(End of file - total 356 lines)
```

OK, I've now read all the relevant files. Let me summarize my understanding and ask the user some clarifying questions.

The "fuzzy" output could be caused by several things:

1. **BMP round-trip changes image type**: When we write a `TYPE_BYTE_GRAY` image as BMP and read it back, Java's BMP ImageReader may return `TYPE_BYTE_INDEXED` instead of `TYPE_BYTE_GRAY`. The `InpsectFactory.createFromImage()` in PDFBox might handle these types differently. For `TYPE_BYTE_INDEXED`, PDFBox would convert it to a 24-bit RGB PNG (lossless but different encoding), while `TYPE_BYTE_GRAY` would be 8-bit grayscale PNG. The visual quality should be identical though.

2. **Mask BMP round-trip**: The mask is `TYPE_BYTE_BINARY` written as BMP. When read back, it might also be `TYPE_BYTE_INDEXED`. `CCITTFactory.createFromImage()` expects a binary image. If the mask type changed, CCITT compression might produce incorrect data. But in the JBIG2 batch path, the mask BMPs are NOT read by Java — they're read by `jbig2enc` directly. So this shouldn't matter for the JBIG2 path.

3. **jbig2enc quality issue**: `jbig2enc` with `-p -s` uses lossless symbol compression. But `-s` (refine) might change symbol appearance if the refine threshold is aggressive. However, `-t 0.5` is the default threshold for symbol dictionary, which is typically conservative enough.

4. **The background image type**: When the `bg` BMP is read in pass 2, it might be a different type than the original grayscale image. In the JBIG2 batch path:
   ```java
   BufferedImage bg = ImageIO.read(new File(tempDir, "bg-" + i + ".bmp"));
   ...
   bgXObject = LosslessFactory.createFromImage(output, background);
   ```
   If the original background was `TYPE_BYTE_GRAY` (from `segmenter.segment(page).getCleanedBackground()`) but the BMP read-back is `TYPE_BYTE_INDEXED`, PDFBox might still handle both correctly. The visual output should be identical.

5. **BMP format resolution**: Actually, wait. The `bg` image is written as BMP before the OCR is submitted. But the OCR reads `page-i.bmp`, not `bg-i.bmp`. So the background image is only read in pass 2 for assembly. The OCR reads the full grayscale page image.

The main change from the previous "known working" version is:
- PNG → BMP for temp files
- Threaded OCR

The PNG → BMP change shouldn't affect visual quality at all (both are lossless formats for the images being stored).

The threaded OCR change shouldn't affect visual quality either (OCR only produces text coordinates, not images).

So what else? The JBIG2 batch path — before this change, the foreground was compressed per-page with jbig2enc (single-page mode). Now it's batch mode with shared symbols. Could batch mode produce worse quality? `jbig2enc -p -s` should produce identical quality to single-page mode, because both use lossless encoding.

Wait — the user ran with `--threads 2`. Let me think about whether different threading behavior could affect the output.

When `workerThreads > 1`, the executor pool has multiple threads. The OCR tasks are submitted immediately in the loop. When workerThreads > 1, multiple page OCRs run concurrently. But by the time we reach pass 2 (assembly), all OCR results are collected (via `Future.get()`). The assembly is sequential and identical regardless of threading.

So the threading change itself shouldn't affect output quality.


So the background goes through: segment → getCleanedBackground() → BMP write → BMP read → LosslessFactory PNG → PDF.

This should be lossless quality. Let me think about what could go wrong with a grayscale image through this pipeline...

Actually, I just thought of something. The `LosslessFactory.createFromImage()` in PDFBox handles `TYPE_BYTE_GRAY` images by encoding them as grayscale PNG. But if the BMP reader returns a `TYPE_BYTE_INDEXED` image with an indexed color model (even if the palette is grayscale), PDFBox might convert it differently. Let me check how PDFBox handles `TYPE_BYTE_INDEXED` in LosslessFactory.

In PDFBox 3.x, `LosslessFactory.createFromImage()` has different code paths for different image types:
- TYPE_BYTE_GRAY: encodes as 8-bit grayscale PNG (grayscale colorspace in PDF)
- TYPE_BYTE_INDEXED: encodes using the indexed color model, creating a DeviceGray or DeviceRGB image based on the palette
- TYPE_INT_RGB: encodes as 24-bit RGB PNG

For `TYPE_BYTE_INDEXED` with a grayscale palette, PDFBox should create a grayscale PNG with the correct pixel data. The visual result should be identical.

OK, another thought: could the issue be that BMP doesn't support `TYPE_BYTE_BINARY` (1-bit) properly? Java's BMP writer might write 1-bit BMPs as 8-bit or 24-bit, losing the binary nature. If the mask BMP is written as 8-bit grayscale, jbig2enc would receive an 8-bit image instead of a 1-bit image. But jbig2enc can handle both — it converts to binary internally using... hmm, actually jbig2enc might use a default threshold (like 0.5) to binarize. This could change the mask appearance!

Wait, but jbig2enc is designed to work with the mask that was ALREADY binarized by our ImageSegmenter. The mask is `TYPE_BYTE_BINARY` — it's already 1-bit (ONLY 0 or 255 pixels). When written as BMP:
- Java's BMP writer for TYPE_BYTE_BINARY: creates a 1-bit BMP (BITMAPINFOHEADER with 1 bit per pixel)
- When read by jbig2enc: jbig2enc reads the BMP header and sees 1-bit, so it processes it as binary

So this should be fine. jbig2enc would receive 1-bit data and encode it losslessly.

OK, I really can't figure out the root cause of the "fuzzy" issue without seeing the actual output or more information. Let me ask the user for clarification.

Let me think about this from another angle. Could the issue be something specific to the user's PDF viewer? Some viewers handle JBIG2 or ImageMask differently. But that's unlikely to be caused by our code changes.

Could the issue be the `IMATE_MASK` + `COLORSPACE` removal? In `addPageJbig2`:
```java
fgImage.getCOSObject().setBoolean(COSName.IMAGE_MASK, true);
fgImage.getCOSObject().removeItem(COSName.COLORSPACE);
```

If the COLORSPACE removal leaves the image without proper rendering parameters, some viewers might render the JBIG2 mask incorrectly. But `IMAGE_MASK=true` makes it a stencil (1-bit transparency), and the `setNonStrokingColor(0,0,0)` sets the stencil color to black. This pattern is standard for PDF transparency masks.

Actually, wait. The `createJbig2ImageXObject(doc, pageData, globalSym, fgWidth, fgHeight)` method (line 230) creates the PDImageXObject without setting IMAGE_MASK. But then in `addPageJbig2` at line 287:
```java
fgImage.getCOSObject().setBoolean(COSName.IMAGE_MASK, true);
fgImage.getCOSObject().removeItem(COSName.COLORSPACE);
```

And at line 289:
```java
cs.setNonStrokingColor(0f, 0f, 0f);
```

This looks correct. IMAGE_MASK makes it a stencil, the black color is set before drawing.

OK I really think I need clarification from the user. Let me ask.

Actually, one more thought: what if the BMP format for the mask changes the mask data? In Java, `ImageIO.write(TYPE_BYTE_BINARY image, "bmp", file)` creates a 1-bit BMP. When read back by Java (`ImageIO.read(file)`), the image might be returned as `TYPE_BYTE_INDEXED` or `TYPE_BYTE_BINARY`. The pixel data is the same either way.

But in the JBIG2 batch path, the mask BMPs are NOT read by Java — they're read by `jbig2enc` directly from disk. So the Java round-trip doesn't apply here.

The `getCleanedBackground()` returns the inpainted background with text removed. If the inpainting quality is poor (leaving text ghosts), the output would look fuzzy.

Based on my reading, there are two distinct issues to address:

**1. Per-page timing includes render+segment time (easy fix)**
- On line 191, `t0` is captured before rendering/segmentation, but the lambda on line 223 uses it as the OCR start time.
- **Fix**: Move the `t0 = System.nanoTime()` inside the lambda, right before `ocrEngine.ocr(pageIdx, tempDir)`.

**2. Fuzzy output — needs diagnosis (harder)**
- The JBIG2 batch path uses `LosslessFactory.createFromImage()` for the background (lossless PNG in PDF) and JBIG2 stencil for the foreground. Both are lossless. The BMP format change (PNG→BMP) should not affect quality since BMP is also lossless.
- I cannot reproduce the fuzziness without the input PDF, and I'm not sure what's causing it.

Let me think about which change could cause "entire page is blurry":

The PNG → BMP change: when we write a BufferedImage as BMP and read it back, the image type might change. Let me think about what types are involved:

1. `BufferedImage page = renderer.renderImageWithDPI(i, dpi)` — returns TYPE_BYTE_GRAY (with RGB image) actually, it depends on the source PDF. For a color input, it returns TYPE_INT_RGB. For grayscale, TYPE_BYTE_GRAY.
2. `BufferedImage gray = toGrayscale(page)` — converts to TYPE_BYTE_GRAY
3. `background = seg.getCleanedBackground()` — returns TYPE_BYTE_GRAY (from the segmentation)
4. `ImageIO.write(background, "bmp", ...)` — writes TYPE_BYTE_GRAY as 8-bit BMP
5. `BufferedImage bg = ImageIO.read(new File(tempDir, "bg-" + i + ".bmp"))` — reads back

The question is: what type does `ImageIO.read()` return for an 8-bit grayscale BMP?

Java's built-in BMP ImageReader will typically return:
- For 1-bit BMP: TYPE_BYTE_BINARY
- For 8-bit BMP with palette: TYPE_BYTE_INDEXED
- For 24-bit BMP: TYPE_INT_RGB

For a TYPE_BYTE_GRAY source image written as BMP, the BMP will be 8-bit with a grayscale palette (256 entries, R=G=B=index). When read back, ImageIO creates a TYPE_BYTE_INDEXED BufferedImage.

Now, `LosslessFactory.createFromImage(output, background)` where background is TYPE_BYTE_INDEXED — how does PDFBox handle this?

In PDFBox 3.x, the `LosslessFactory` handles image types:
- TYPE_INT_RGB, TYPE_INT_ARGB: 24/32 bit PNG
- TYPE_BYTE_GRAY: 8-bit grayscale PNG
- TYPE_BYTE_BINARY: 1-bit PNG
- TYPE_BYTE_INDEXED: 8-bit indexed PNG (or expanded to RGB?)

Let me check: PDFBox's `LosslessFactory.createFromImage()` in version 3.0.x...

Actually, I don't have access to the PDFBox source code here. But I recall that PDFBox's LosslessFactory handles TYPE_BYTE_INDEXED by encoding it with the color model data as a PNG. The indexed color model with a grayscale palette should produce a grayscale PNG that looks identical.

But wait — maybe the issue is that the indexed color model is not preserved correctly through the BMP-PNG pipeline? If the indexed palette has slight deviations from pure gray, the image might look slightly different.

No, that's unlikely. The palette in the BMP is exactly the grayscale values (0,0,0), (1,1,1), ..., (255,255,255).

Let me think about another possibility: PDFBox's `LosslessFactory.createFromImage()` for TYPE_BYTE_INDEXED images. In recent PDFBox versions (3.0.x), it might convert the image to RGB first and then encode as 24-bit PNG. This would look the same but be 3x larger in the PDF (before compression). But the compression (Deflate on PNG) would make the 24-bit version about the same size as the 8-bit version for smooth images.

Actually, that's still the same visual quality.

<details>
<summary>Thinking</summary>

Now I see a critical problem! The output PDF has:
- Background images: `rgb` type (3 components, 8 bits per component) — these are COLOR images
- Stencil (foreground): jbig2 encoded, 1-bit stencil

The background images should be GRAYSCALE, not RGB! The background is coming through as RGB (color!) even though we're converting to grayscale.

Wait — let me think about this more carefully. The background image pipeline is:
1. `renderImageWithDPI(i, dpi)` — renders the PDF page as a BufferedImage
2. `toGrayscale(page)` — converts to TYPE_BYTE_GRAY
3. `segmenter.segment(page)` — segments the grayscale image 
4. `seg.getCleanedBackground()` — gets background (should be TYPE_BYTE_GRAY)
5. `ImageIO.write(background, "bmp", ...)` — writes BMP
6. `ImageIO.read(new File(tempDir, "bg-" + i + ".bmp"))` — reads BMP
7. `LosslessFactory.createFromImage(output, background)` — creates PDImageXObject

The `LosslessFactory.createFromImage()` for a TYPE_BYTE_GRAY or TYPE_BYTE_INDEXED image should create a grayscale image in the PDF. But `pdfimages -list` shows `rgb` type. This means the image is being stored as RGB (24-bit) instead of grayscale (8-bit).

This could be because:
1. The BMP reader returns TYPE_INT_RGB instead of TYPE_BYTE_GRAY
2. The `LosslessFactory` converts the image to RGB for some reason

If the background is stored as RGB in the PDF, the file size would be 3× larger for the uncompressed data. But after Deflate compression, the actual file size might not be 3× larger (gray images compress well in RGB too).

But the visual quality should be identical — RGB vs grayscale for a gray image should look the same.

Hmm, but the background images being stored as RGB is NOT necessarily the issue. It would just be larger file size (and we see 4.2 MB for 10 pages, which is reasonable).

OK wait — the key finding is: the output PDF has image and stencil pairs, which means JBIG2 batch path is working. The background is `rgb` type — this might be because `LosslessFactory.createFromImage()` always creates RGB images for non-TYPE_BYTE_GRAY input.

Let me think about what's causing the "fuzzy" appearance. Let me extract one of the background images from the PDF.

Actually, let me extract the images and compare them to the original page to see if they're blurry.

</details>

If the inpainting is poor (smearing text over a large area), the image would look "fuzzy" — blurry text areas where the text was removed. This is a characteristic of MRC output: the background has text removed (smoothed over), and the foreground stencil re-adds the text. Some viewers might not render the stencil correctly, or the stencil might not perfectly align with the background, creating a "ghost" effect.

But the user says "previously worked" — so this is a regression. Let me check what change could cause the visual quality to degrade.

Wait, I just realized something. The background image type is `rgb` in the PDF — 24-bit color. But the original images are grayscale (TYPE_BYTE_GRAY). If `LosslessFactory.createFromImage()` stores grayscale images as grayscale in the PDF (8-bit DeviceGray), then we'd see `gray` type. But we see `rgb`. This means something in the pipeline is converting the grayscale image to color.

The step where this conversion would happen is in pass 2: `BufferedImage bg = ImageIO.read(new File(tempDir, "bg-" + i + ".bmp"))`. If this reads the BMP as a color image (TYPE_INT_RGB) instead of grayscale, then `LosslessFactory.createFromImage()` would produce an RGB image.

But actually, this is just a storage format issue and doesn't affect quality. Whether the PDF stores the background as DeviceGray or DeviceRGB, the pixel values are the same (for a gray image, R=G=B=gray_value).

So the images are correct size for 300 DPI.

<details>
<summary>Thinking</summary>

Now I see several important things:

1. **output-mrc.pdf** (4.6 MB) — CCITT foreground, jbig2 has NOT been applied (ccitt instead of jbig2). Wait, it uses CCITT, so JBIG2 was not used (or fell back to CCITT). Actually, this might be from before JBIG2 was implemented — the older version.

2. **output-no-mrc.pdf** (9.2 MB) — JPEG background, NO stencil, NO foreground mask. The images are JPEG encoded at 800-1125 KB each. This is the non-MRC path (background only).

3. **output.pdf** (4.2 MB) — the LATEST version with JBIG2 batch compression. JBIG2 stencils (2.7-4.2 KB each) + Lossless RGB backgrounds (315-505 KB each). The total is 4.2 MB which is slightly less than output-mrc.pdf.

The key observation: **ALL THREE versions have `rgb` type backgrounds**. The non-MRC version (JPEG) also has RGB images. So the background image type is NOT new.

But the extracted text is the same garbled quality across all versions. And the # of text lines is the same (7063). So the OCR is producing identical results.

Now, the question is: what's different about the latest output.pdf that makes it "fuzzy" when the user says previous versions worked?

One big difference: in output-mrc.pdf, the stencil is CCITT (30-45 KB per page). In output.pdf, the stencil is JBIG2 (2.7-4.2 KB per page). JBIG2 is a lossy or lossless encoding. With `-p -s` flags, jbig2enc does LOSSY symbol refinement by default. If symbols are merged incorrectly (e.g., two different characters get the same symbol), the stencil would show incorrect character shapes, leading to "fuzzy" text.

Wait, `-p -s` means:
- `-p`: use refinement (symbol + refinement coding, not text region)
- `-s`: use symbol coding (text region, not generic)

Actually, let me check: `-p` is for "page as image" (generic refinement), `-s` is for "symbol coding". When used together, `-s` is for symbol-based compression which IS lossy by default (it classifies symbols and may merge similar but different glyphs).

Actually, `jbig2enc` with `-s` alone is lossy (it clusters similar symbols). To get lossless encoding, you need `--lossless` or `-S` (symbol coding with no refinement).

But wait — for a binary text mask from our ImageSegmenter, the mask contains ONLY text pixels (black) on a white background. The characters are perfectly separated. `jbig2enc -s` would find the symbols and encode them. The lossy part of `-s` is in the symbol matching — if two different characters look similar (e.g., "c" and "e"), they might be merged. This would make some characters look wrong (the wrong shape or fuzzy).

However, the user said "entire page is blurry", not that individual characters look wrong. This suggests the issue is with the background image, not the foreground stencil.

Actually, wait — let me reconsider. The user said "entire page is blurry" which is the observation they made about the PDF when viewed. If the foreground mask IS correct and sharp, the blurriness would have to come from the background.

Let me think about what could make the background blurry:
1. The `LosslessFactory.createFromImage()` is creating a PDF image that looks blurry when rendered
2. The BMP round-trip degrades the image
3. The JPEGFactory.createFromImage() with 0.85 quality (for non-MRC path)

But wait — the JBIG2 batch path uses `LosslessFactory.createFromImage(output, background)` where `background` is `ImageIO.read(new File(tempDir, "bg-" + i + ".bmp"))`. 

LosslessFactory creates a PNG-based image. The visual quality of a lossless PNG image should be identical to the source image. 

Unless... the image type change (TYPE_BYTE_INDEXED instead of TYPE_BYTE_GRAY) causes PDFBox to use a different code path that results in quality loss.

Actually, let me test this theory. Let me write a small program to check what image type the BMP reader returns for a grayscale BMP.

Or even simpler — let me just compare the image quality using ImageMagick or similar tools.

Actually, let me try a totally different approach. Let me convert the output PDF to images (using pdftoppm) and compare with the source.

</details>

<details>
<summary>Thinking</summary>

There it is!

**"Invalid segment reference in JBIG2 text region"** — pdftoppm (poppler) reports JBIG2 decoding errors for EVERY page of the output.pdf! This is the root cause!

The JBIG2 data generated by `jbig2enc -p -s` has "Invalid segment references" — meaning the per-page segment data references segments that don't exist or are malformed.

The 4-digit error messages like "(379259)" are file offsets. The "2709 extraneous bytes after segment" means the JBIG2 decoder found extra bytes that didn't match any known segment type.

This is likely the Bug 1 regression! The `-b basename` flag creates output files named `<basename>.0000`, `<basename>.0001`, etc. But the `-p -s` flags with `-b` might create different output than expected. Let me think about this...

When jbig2enc runs with `-p -s -b <basename>`, it:
1. Creates `<basename>.sym` — global symbol dictionary
2. Creates `<basename>.0000` — first page data
3. Creates `<basename>.0001` — second page data (if multiple pages)

BUT — each `.0000` file references the `.sym` file's segment by number. If the segment reference doesn't match (different segment numbers), the decoder fails.

Actually, the issue might be simpler. Let me check the compressAllFromDir code to see how we collect the output from jbig2enc.

</details>

```
97:      *         jbig2enc is unavailable or fails (caller should use CCITT fallback).
98:      */
99:     public BatchResult compressAllFromDir(File maskDir, int pageCount, int[] imgWidths, int[] imgHeights) throws IOException {
100:         File jbig2enc = findJbig2enc();
101:         if (jbig2enc == null) return null;
102: 
103:         List<String> imagePaths = new ArrayList<>(pageCount);
104:         for (int i = 0; i < pageCount; i++) {
105:             imagePaths.add(new File(maskDir, "mask-" + i + ".bmp").getAbsolutePath());
106:         }
107: 
108:         // Use the first mask file's path (without .bmp) as the basename so that
109:         // jbig2enc writes deterministic output files alongside the masks:
110:         //   <basename>.sym  — shared global symbol dictionary
111:         //   <basename>.0000, <basename>.0001, ... — per-page JBIG2 data
112:         String firstImage = imagePaths.get(0);
113:         String basename = firstImage.replaceAll("\\.bmp$", "");
114: 
115:         String flags = Settings.getInstance().getString("jbig2enc.flags", "-p -s");
116:         List<String> args = new ArrayList<>();
117:         for (String f : flags.split("\\s+")) {
118:             if (!f.isEmpty()) args.add(f);
119:         }
120:         args.add("-b");
121:         args.add(basename);
122:         args.addAll(imagePaths);
123: 
124:         SubprocessRunner.Result result = SubprocessRunner.run(jbig2enc, args, -1);
125:         if (result.getExitCode() != 0) return null;
126: 
127:         // Read shared global symbol dictionary
128:         File symFile = new File(basename + ".sym");
129:         byte[] globalSym = symFile.exists() ? Files.readAllBytes(symFile.toPath()) : new byte[0];
130:         if (globalSym.length == 0) return null;
131: 
132:         // Read per-page data (jbig2enc -p writes <basename>.0000, .0001, ...)
133:         List<CompressionResult> pages = new ArrayList<>(pageCount);
134:         for (int i = 0; i < pageCount; i++) {
135:             File pageFile = new File(basename + "." + String.format("%04d", i));
136:             byte[] pageData = pageFile.exists() ? Files.readAllBytes(pageFile.toPath()) : new byte[0];
137:             pages.add(new CompressionResult(pageData, imgWidths[i], imgHeights[i], true));
138:         }
139: 
140:         return new BatchResult(globalSym, pages);
141:     }
142: 
143:     /**
144:      * Attempts JBIG2 compression via jbig2enc; falls back to CCITT G4 on failure.
145:      *
146:      * @param foregroundMask  Binary image (TYPE_BYTE_BINARY, black=foreground).
147:      * @return CompressionResult with either JBIG2 or CCITT G4 data.
148:      */
149:     public CompressionResult compress(BufferedImage foregroundMask) throws IOException {
150:         File jbig2enc = findJbig2enc();
151:         if (jbig2enc != null) {
152:             try {
153:                 return compressWithJbig2enc(jbig2enc, foregroundMask);
154:             } catch (IOException e) {
155:                 // jbig2enc failed → fall through to fallback
156:             }
157:         }
158:         return compressWithFallback(foregroundMask);
159:     }
160: 
161:     /**
162:      * Locates the jbig2enc binary for the current OS.
163:      *
164:      * Searches native/{os}/jbig2enc (or jbig2enc.exe on Windows).
165:      * The base path can be overridden via system property "tfocr.native.dir"
166:      * (defaults to "native").
167:      *
168:      * @return The executable File, or null if not found.
169:      */
170:     private File findJbig2enc() {
171:         Os os = PlatformUtils.detectOs();
172:         String dirName;
173:         switch (os) {
174:             case WINDOWS: dirName = "win"; break;
175:             case MAC: dirName = "mac"; break;
176:             default: dirName = "linux";
177:         }
178:         File exe = new File(nativeDir + "/" + dirName, os == Os.WINDOWS ? "jbig2enc.exe" : "jbig2enc");
179:         if (exe.canExecute()) return exe;
180:         return null;
181:     }
182: 
183:     /**
184:      * Runs jbig2enc and concatenates global sym + page jbig2 into one byte array.
185:      *
186:      * PDF JBIG2Decode streams require global segments (symbol dictionary)
187:      * followed by page data — exactly the .sym + .jbig2 layout that
188:      * jbig2enc produces.
189:      */
190:     private CompressionResult compressWithJbig2enc(File jbig2enc, BufferedImage mask) throws IOException {
191:         File tempBmp = File.createTempFile("tfocr-jbig2-", ".bmp");
192:         tempBmp.deleteOnExit();
193:         javax.imageio.ImageIO.write(mask, "bmp", tempBmp);
194: 
195:         try {
196:             String prefix = tempBmp.getAbsolutePath().replaceAll("\\.bmp$", "");
197: 
198:             String flags = Settings.getInstance().getString("jbig2enc.flags", "-p -s");
199:             java.util.List<String> args = new java.util.ArrayList<>();
200:             for (String f : flags.split("\\s+")) {
201:                 if (!f.isEmpty()) args.add(f);
202:             }
203:             args.add("-b");
204:             args.add(prefix);
205:             args.add(tempBmp.getAbsolutePath());
206: 
207:             SubprocessRunner.Result result = SubprocessRunner.run(jbig2enc, args, -1);
208:             if (result.getExitCode() != 0) {
209:                 throw new IOException("jbig2enc failed: " + result.getStderrString());
210:             }
211: 
212:             // With -p flag, jbig2enc produces <prefix>.sym + <prefix>.0000
213:             File symFile = new File(prefix + ".sym");
214:             File pageFile = new File(prefix + ".0000");
215: 
216:             byte[] symData = symFile.exists() ? java.nio.file.Files.readAllBytes(symFile.toPath()) : new byte[0];
217:             byte[] pageData = pageFile.exists() ? java.nio.file.Files.readAllBytes(pageFile.toPath()) : new byte[0];
218: 
219:             byte[] combined = new byte[symData.length + pageData.length];
220:             System.arraycopy(symData, 0, combined, 0, symData.length);
221:             System.arraycopy(pageData, 0, combined, symData.length, pageData.length);
222: 
223:             return new CompressionResult(combined, mask.getWidth(), mask.getHeight(), true);
224:         } finally {
225:             String prefix = tempBmp.getAbsolutePath().replaceAll("\\.bmp$", "");
226:             for (String ext : new String[]{".bmp", ".sym", ".0000"}) {
227:                 File f = new File(prefix + ext);
228:                 if (f.exists()) f.delete();
229:             }
230:         }
231:     }
232: 
233:     /**
234:      * Fallback: encodes the mask as CCITT Group 4 fax using PDFBox's CCITTFactory.
235:      */
236:     private CompressionResult compressWithFallback(BufferedImage mask) throws IOException {
237:         // Create a throwaway PDDocument to use CCITTFactory
238:         try (PDDocument doc = new PDDocument()) {
239:             PDImageXObject ccitt = CCITTFactory.createFromImage(doc, mask);
240:             return new CompressionResult(extractStreamBytes(ccitt), mask.getWidth(), mask.getHeight(), false);
241:         }
242:     }
243: 
244:     private static byte[] extractStreamBytes(PDImageXObject image) throws IOException {
245:         try (java.io.InputStream is = image.createInputStream()) {
246:             return is.readAllBytes();
247:         }
248:     }
249: }

(End of file - total 249 lines)
</content>
```

<details>
<summary>Thinking</summary>

Found the root cause!

The `compressAllFromDir` method uses a basename like:
```
/path/to/temp/mask-0.bmp → basename = /path/to/temp/mask-0
```

Then jbig2enc writes:
```
/path/to/temp/mask-0.sym
/path/to/temp/mask-0.0000
/path/to/temp/mask-0.0001
...
```

The `symFile` and `pageFile` are read correctly from the right paths.

But wait, the pdftoppm error says "Invalid segment reference in JBIG2 text region" and "X extraneous bytes after segment". This means the JBIG2 data is malformed — the per-page segment makes references to symbol dictionary segments that don't exist or are in the wrong position.

The issue is likely with how `jbig2enc ` encodes with `-p -s`:
1. The global `.sym` file contains the shared symbol dictionary
2. Each `.0000`, `.0001` file contains the page data with references to the global symbol

When PDFBox embeds the page data as a raw JBIG2 stream and the global symbol dictionary as a separate `/JBIG2Globals` stream, the decoder reads them in the order: first the globals, then the page data. The page data references symbols by their segment number in the global dictionary.

Now, jbig2enc `-p -s` creates:
- `.sym`: Contains a symbol dictionary segment (segment type 0) and possibly a refinement/aggregate segment
- `.0000`: Contains a page information segment (segment type 48) and a text region segment (segment type 42) that references the symbol dictionary

The segment number in the `.0000` file's text region references the symbol dictionary segment. If the segment number is 0 (the first segment in the file), it should match correctly.

But wait — the global `.sym` file and the `.0000` file are SEPARATE files. JBIG2 page data expects the global segments to come BEFORE the page segments in the stream. When PDFBox feeds the JBIG2 decoder: first `/JBIG2Globals` data (from `.sym`), then the page data (from `.0000`). The combined stream should be: [global sym data] + [page data].

In the single-page path (`compressWithJbig2enc`), the combined data IS concatenated:
```java
byte[] combined = new byte[symData.length + pageData.length];
System.arraycopy(symData, 0, combined, 0, symData.length);
System.arraycopy(pageData, 0, combined, symData.length, pageData.length);
```

But in the batch path (`compressAllFromDir`), the globals and page data are stored SEPARATELY:
- `globalSym` = contents of `.sym`
- `pageData` = contents of `.0000`

And in PDFAssembler, they're stored as separate streams:
- `/JBIG2Globals` = globalSym
- JBIG2Decode stream = pageData

This is the CORRECT way to do it per the PDF spec. The JBIG2Globals stream contains the global segments, and each page's stream contains just the page-specific segments. The decoder should concatenate them internally.

So the issue is NOT with the storage. The issue is that the JBIG2 data itself is somehow corrupt.

Let me check what jbig2enc actually produces. Let me run jbig2enc on one of the mask files and inspect the output.

Actually, let me think about this more carefully. The error messages are:
- "Invalid segment reference in JBIG2 text region" — the text region (segment type 42) references a symbol dictionary segment by number, but that segment number doesn't exist in the combined stream.
- "X extraneous bytes after segment" — after parsing all known segments, there are leftover bytes.

The "extraneous bytes" issue suggests that the page data file has trailing bytes that don't form valid JBIG2 segments. This could happen if jbig2enc appends padding or if the `.0000` file format is different from what we expect.

Actually, let me test this directly — let me run jbig2enc on a mask image and inspect the output.

</details>

OK, so jbig2enc produces:
- `.0000`: 70 bytes — this is the page data
- `.sym`: 43 bytes — this is the symbol dictionary

Now I want to understand whether `.0000` file alone (without the `.sym`) is valid JBIG2 data. The PDF spec says the JBIG2Globals stream provides the global data, and the page stream provides the page data. The decoder concatenates them.

But looking at the `.0000` hex dump — let me parse the JBIG2 segments:

The first bytes `00 00 00 01` — this is the segment number (1). In JBIG2, segments are numbered sequentially.

Wait, looking more carefully:
- `.sym` — contains the symbol dictionary segment (segment number 0 typically)
- `.0000` — contains the page data segments that reference segment 0

Let me check the `.sym` file content.

### Tool: bash

**Input:**

```json
{
  "command": "cd /tmp && python3 -c \"\nwith open('test_basename.sym', 'rb') as f:\n    data = f.read()\nprint(f'.sym hexdump ({len(data)} bytes):')\nfor i in range(0, len(data), 16):\n    hex_str = ' '.join(f'{b:02x}' for b in data[i:i+16])\n    print(f'  {i:04x}: {hex_str}')\n\n# Parse JBIG2 segments\n# Segment header: 4 bytes segment number, 1 byte segment type, 4 bytes page number (big-endian), ...\nimport struct\ndef parse_jbig2_segment(data, offset, label):\n    if offset >= len(data):\n        return offset, None\n    seg_num = struct.unpack('>I', data[offset:offset+4])[0]\n    seg_type = data[offset+4]\n    page = struct.unpack('>I', data[offset+5:offset+9])[0]\n    # Segment type decoding\n    type_names = {0: 'SymbolDict', 4: 'TextRegion', 6: 'RefineRegion', 7: 'LineRegion',\n                  20: 'PatternDict', 38: 'HalftoneRegion', 40: 'GenericRegion',\n                  48: 'PageInfo', 49: 'EndOfPage', 50: 'Tables', 51: 'Extension',\n                  52: 'Profiles', 53: 'CodeTable'}\n    tname = type_names.get(seg_type, f'Unknown({seg_type})')\n    print(f'{label} seg#{seg_num} type={tname} page={page}')\n    return offset + 9, None\n\nparse_jbig2_segment(data, 0, 'page.sym')\n\" 2>&1",
```

<details>
<summary>Thinking</summary>

OK, the `.sym` file contains segment number 0 (SymbolDict). The page is `00 00 00 00` = 0, not 16777216. Let me look at this more carefully.

Actually wait, the segment number is 4 bytes: `00 00 00 00`. The segment type is `00` which is... hmm, JBIG2 segment types are documented. Let me check:

Segment type 0 = Symbol Dictionary (segment type bits 0-5 = 0)
Segment type flags: the segment type byte includes the segment type in the lower 6 bits and flags in the upper 2 bits.

Wait, let me reparse: `00 00 00 00 00` — segment number 0, then byte `00` is the segment type. But `00` as a segment type... let me check JBIG2 spec.

Actually in JBIG2, segment types use these constants:
- 0 = Symbol Dictionary
- 4 = Intermediate Text Region  
- 6 = Intermediate Refinement Region
- 7 = Intermediate Line Region
- 20 = halftone (pattern) dictionary
- 38 = halftone (pattern) region
- 40 = generic region
- 48 = page information
- 49 = end of page
- 50 = tables
- 51 = extension
- 52 = profiles
- 53 = code table

So `00` = Symbol Dictionary. The next byte after that should be the page association:

Bytes: `00 00 00 00 00` (seg #0, seg type 0 = SymbolDict)
Next: `01 00 00 00` — page association: bit 7 of type byte... wait.

Actually, the segment header format is:
- 4 bytes: segment number (big-endian, unsigned 32-bit)
- 1 byte: segment type flags (bits 7 = defer, bit 6 = count segment, bits 5-0 = type)
- 4 bytes: page association (big-endian, unsigned 32-bit; or if bit 7 of segment type is set, it's a 1-byte page number)
- 4 bytes: segment data length (big-endian, unsigned 32-bit)
- 1 byte: number of referenced segments (unsigned)
- Then for each referenced segment: 4 bytes (big-endian, segment number)

Let me reparse:

`00 00 00 00` — segment number 0
`00` — segment type: bits 5-0 = 0 (SymbolDict), bit 6 = 0 (not count), bit 7 = 0 (page assoc is 4 bytes)
`01 00 00 00` — page association = 1 (wait, that would be big-endian... `00 00 00 01`)

Actually wait, let me be more careful:
`00 00 00 00 00 01 00 00 00 00` 

Byte 0-3: `00 00 00 00` = segment number 0
Byte 4: `00` = segment type byte. Bits 5-0 = 0 -> SymbolDict. Bit 7 = 0 -> 4-byte page number.
Byte 5-8: `01 00 00 00` — but wait, this should be page association in big-endian. Let me check the spec more carefully...

From the JBIG2 spec (ISO/IEC 14492), the segment header is:
- Segment number: big-endian UInt32
- Segment header flags: bits 7 (page_association_size: 0 = 4 bytes, 1 = 1 byte), bit 6 (segment_retention), bits 5-0 (segment type)
- Referred-to segment count: byte + variable

Hmm, actually looking at the JBIG2 standard more carefully:

Segment header:
1. Segment number (4 bytes, unsigned big-endian)
2. Segment type (1 byte): 
   - Bits 7-6: 00 = retains, 01 = not retained (page association is 1 byte), 10 = not retained (page association is 4 bytes)
   
Wait, I'm getting confused. Let me just look at what poppler's JBIG2 decoder complains about. The error is "Invalid segment reference in JBIG2 text region". This means the text region segment references a symbol dictionary segment that doesn't exist.

But looking at the `.0000` hex:
```
00 00 00 01 30 00 01 00 00 00 13 00 00 00 64 00
```

But wait, I was wrong about the JBIG2 header structure. Let me look at the actual JBIG2 spec.

The segment header:
- segment_number (4 bytes, big-endian)
- segment_header_flags (1 byte):
  - bit 7: page_association_size (0 = 4 bytes, 1 = 1 byte)  
  - bit 6: segment_retention_flag (0 = retain, 1 = do not retain)
  - bit 5: if set, segment type is "end of file"

Wait, actually the segment type is 6 bits (bits 5-0), and bit 5 (value 32) is part of the type. Let me reconsider:

The segment header flags byte format (from the JBIG2 spec):
- Bit 7: page_association_size
- Bit 6: segment_retention_flag
- Bits 5-0: segment_type

- bit 7 = 0: page_association is 4 bytes
- bit 6 = 0: retain segment  
- bits 5-0 = 0b110000 = 48: Page Information segment

Hmm no, 0b110000 = 48 decimal. But segment type 48 in JBIG2 is... let me check.

From the JBIG2 spec, the segment types:
0 = Symbol Dictionary
1-3 = reserved
4 = Intermediate Text Region  
5 = reserved
6 = Intermediate Refinement Region
7 = Intermediate Line Region
8-15 = reserved
16 = Intermediate Halftone Region  — actually no, these aren't right.

Let me look at a reliable source. The Segment type codes from the JBIG2 standard (ISO/IEC 14492):
- 0: Symbol dictionary
- 4: Intermediate text region
- 6: Intermediate refinement region  
- 7: Intermediate line region
- 20: Pattern dictionary (halftone)
- 38: Intermediate halftone region
- 40: Generic region
- 42: Text region
- 43: Refinement region
- 44: Line region
- 48: Page information (0x30)
- 49: End of page (0x31)
- 50: Tables (0x32)
- 51: Extension (0x33)
- 52: Profiles (0x34)
- 53: Code table (0x35)

So 48 (0x30) = Page Information. Good.

```json
{
```

OK, the parsing shows:

These values are COMPLETELY wrong. Page=65536 doesn't make sense, and data_length=1245184 is way larger than the 114-byte file. So my parsing of the segment structure is incorrect.

Let me look at this differently. The standard says:

Segment Header (from ISO 14492):
```
Segment number (4 bytes, big-endian unsigned integer)
Segment header flags (1 byte):
  Bit 7: page_association_size (0=4 bytes, 1=1 byte)
  Bit 6: segment_retention_flags (reserved, should be 0)
  Bits 5-0: segment type
Page association (4 bytes if page_association_size=0, else 1 byte)
Segment data length (4 bytes, big-endian)
Referred-to segment count (1 byte, or 4 bytes if first byte = 255)
  If count = 255, then next 4 bytes are the actual count
```
Then for each referred-to segment: 4-byte big-endian segment number

Then the segment data of `data_length` bytes.

But my parsing is clearly wrong. Let me look at the hex:
```
00 00 00 01 30 00 01 00 00 00 13 00 00 00 64 00

00 02 ...
```

`00 00 00 01` = segment number 1

Wait, what if the segment type flags are NOT encoding page_assoc_size as bit 7? Let me look at the actual JBIG2 spec description of the segment header flags:

From the PDF 32000-1:2008 spec (Annex H, which references JBIG2):
"In the JBIG2 standard, the segment header includes a page association field whose size is determined by bit 7 of the segment type flag byte"

So bit 7 determines the page association size. With `30` = 0x30 = 0b0011_0000, bit 7 = 0, so 4 bytes.

I think the issue is that my tool `pdfimages` output shows the stencil is encoded as JBIG2, and when `pdftoppm` renders it, it produces "Invalid segment reference" errors. This means the JBIG2 data is somehow invalid when embedded in the PDF.


The first error "Invalid segment reference" suggests the text region references symbol dictionary segment 0, but segment 0 is in the `/JBIG2Globals` stream, not in the page stream. The page stream contains segment 1+ which has the text region that references segment 0. The JBIG2 decoder should look at the concatenated globals + page stream, so segment 0 should be available.

But wait — maybe the `/JBIG2Globals` key is not being set correctly? Or maybe the page data file DOES include its own symbol dictionary (redundantly) and the reference is wrong?

Actually, now I'm wondering: does `jbig2enc -p -s` embed a REFERENCE to the global symbol dictionary in the page data? The page data's text region would reference segment number 0 (the symbol dictionary). When the PDF decoder sees the page data, it looks for segment 0 in the concatenated globals+page stream. Since globals come first, segment 0 should be found.

Let me just check whether the `.0000` file by itself is valid (without the globals). If jbig2enc puts the references relative to the complete stream, the `.0000` would reference segment 0 (which exists in `.sym`) and would fail without it. This is expected.

But the PDF viewer should concatenate globals+page streams. If the concatenated stream is valid, there should be no errors.

Unless... the way PDFBox stores the JBIG2 data causes the decoder to misinterpret it. Let me check how `addPageJbig2` stores the data.

Looking at PDFAssembler.java line 285-290:
```java
PDImageXObject fgImage = createJbig2ImageXObject(output,
    jbig2PageData, jbig2GlobalSym, fgWidth, fgHeight);
fgImage.getCOSObject().setBoolean(COSName.IMAGE_MASK, true);
fgImage.getCOSObject().removeItem(COSName.COLORSPACE);
cs.setNonStrokingColor(0f, 0f, 0f);
cs.drawImage(fgImage, 0, 0, pageW, pageH);
```

And `createJbig2ImageXObject` (line 230):
```java
private PDImageXObject createJbig2ImageXObject(PDDocument doc,
                                                byte[] pageData,
                                                byte[] globalSym,
                                                int width,
                                                int height) throws IOException {
    PDImageXObject img = new PDImageXObject(doc);
    img.setWidth(width);
    img.setHeight(height);
    img.setBitsPerComponent(1);
    img.setStencil(true);

    // Write per-page JBIG2 data
    try (OutputStream os = img.getStream().createOutputStream()) {
        os.write(pageData);
    }
    img.getCOSObject().setItem(COSName.FILTER, COSName.JBIG2_DECODE);

    // Create the shared global symbol dictionary stream
    if (cachedJbig2Globals == null) {
        cachedJbig2Globals = doc.getDocument().createCOSStream();
        try (OutputStream os = cachedJbig2Globals.createOutputStream()) {
            os.write(globalSym);
        }
    }
    img.getCOSObject().setItem(COSName.JBIG2_GLOBALS, cachedJbig2Globals);

    return img;
}
```

Wait, there's a potential bug here. Let me look at this code path more carefully.

The `globalSym` comes from `compressAllFromDir` which reads `<basename>.sym`. The `pageData` comes from `<basename>.0000` (or `.0001`, etc.).

The `.sym` file is supposed to contain ONLY the shared symbol dictionary segment (segment 0). The `.0000` file is supposed to contain only the page-specific segments (segment 1+).

When combined, the decoder sees: [segment 0 from .sym] + [segment 1 from .0000]. The text region in segment 1 references segment 0 (the symbol dictionary) which is available in the combined stream.

But looking at the `.0000` file structure — let me look at what segments it contains:

Parsing the `.0000` bytes with the CORRECT header structure. I need to determine the page_assoc and data_length correctly.

Let me look at the binary layout of a Page Information segment (type 48):

The Page Information segment data contains:
- width (4 bytes, big-endian)
- height (4 bytes, big-endian)
- resolution (4 bytes, big-endian, x)
- resolution (4 bytes, big-endian, y)
- flags (1 byte)

That's 17 bytes of data.

After the Page Information segment, the next segment should be a Text Region (type 42).

Let me parse the hex dump more carefully:
```
00 00 00 01 30 00 01 00 00 00 13 00 00 00 64 00
00 00 c8 00 00 00 00 00 00 00 00 00 00 00 00 00
00 02 00 00 01 00 00 00 20 00 00 03 ff fd ff 02
fe fe fe 00 00 00 01 00 00 00 01 24 23 0c b2 0d
a0 9f 79 1f ff 71 cf ff ac 00 00 00 03 06 42 00
02 01 00 00 00 1c 00 00 00 64 00 00 00 c8 00 00
00 00 00 00 00 00 00 00 00 00 00 00 01 9e 0b 23
ff ac
```

Let me try a different parse where data_length starts at byte 9:

`00 00 00 01` = seg# = 1
`30` = flags (type=48=PageInfo)
`00 01 00 00` — if this is page_assoc, then big-endian = 65536, which is wrong

OR maybe the page_assoc is NOT big-endian? Maybe jbig2enc uses little-endian for page_assoc?

`00 01 00 00` as little-endian = 256

That's still not right.

Wait — what if the page_association_size flag is interpreted differently? In PDF 32000, section H.3: "The page_association_size flag in the segment header determines the size of the page association field: a value of 0 indicates a 4-byte field, a value of 1 indicates a 1-byte field."

But what if the jbig2enc code encodes the page_association_size flag in the OPPOSITE way? What if they use 0 for 1-byte and 1 for 4-byte?

Let me try: if bit 7 = 0 means 1-byte page association:
`30` → bit 7 = 0 → 1-byte page assoc
Then page_assoc = `00` = page 0 (but should be page 1 for a page segment, not page 0)

Hmm, or what if the rule is different?

Actually, you know what, let me just look at what the JBIG2 standard says. In the actual JBIG2 spec:

"Segment page association (4 bytes or 1 byte depending on the page_association_size flag in the segment header, a byte indicating the page number)"

Hmm, that's the same.

OK let me try a completely different approach. Let me take the actual data from output.pdf and feed it to `jbig2dec` (or a JBIG2 decoder) to see if the data is valid.

Actually, I think the real issue might be that the `.0000` file from `jbig2enc -p -s` already INCLUDES its own copy of the symbol data, and the `.sym` file is redundant. When stored as separate streams in the PDF, the decoder concatenates both, getting duplicate symbol definitions, which might cause "invalid segment reference" errors because segment numbers get confused.

But wait, that doesn't match the error either. Let me just run the test more carefully.

Actually, I just had another idea. Maybe the bug is that `compressAllFromDir` reads the `.0000` file correctly, but the `.0000` file by itself is NOT a valid JBIG2 stream because it references segment 0 (the symbol dictionary) which is in the `.sym` file. When embedded in the PDF, the page data references segment 0, and the JBIG2Globals provides segment 0. The decoder should concatenate them.

BUT — what if the `.0000` file starts with segment number 1 (the Page Information segment), and then has a Text Region that references segment 0? The JBIG2Globals contains segment 0. When concatenated, the full stream would have segment 0 (from globals) followed by segment 1 (from page data). The text region references segment 0, which is available. This should work.

UNLESS the segment number in the page data is NOT 1 but something else. Let me look at the actual bytes more carefully.

Actually, let me just try to run jbig2dec or equivalent on the .sym + .0000 concatenated data.

Oh wait, I checked earlier: jbig2dec is not installed. Let me install it or use another approach.

Actually, let me just use mutool to inspect the PDF's JBIG2 streams.

Wait, I should focus on the most likely cause. Let me think about what changed in the JBIG2 batch path:

1. Before: `compressWithJbig2enc` was called per page, which combines `.sym` + `.0000` into a single byte array. The combined data is stored as a single JBIG2 stream (no `/JBIG2Globals`).

2. After: `compressAllFromDir` stores `.sym` and `.0000` separately. The `.sym` goes to `/JBIG2Globals`, the `.0000` goes to the page stream.

Could the issue be in how `createOutputStream` works vs `createOutputStream` with COSStream filters?

In `compressWithJbig2enc` (old, single-page per invocation):
```java
// Combines sym + page data
byte[] combined = new byte[symData.length + pageData.length];
System.arraycopy(symData, 0, combined, 0, symData.length);
System.arraycopy(pageData, 0, combined, symData.length, pageData.length);
// ...

    os.write(result.getData());

```

In `createJbig2ImageXObject` (new, batch path):
```java
try (OutputStream os = img.getStream().createOutputStream()) {
    os.write(pageData);
}
img.getCOSObject().setItem(COSName.FILTER, COSName.JBIG2_DECODE);
```

Wait, both use `createOutputStream()` without any filter. So neither should apply additional compression.

Let me check: does `COSStream.createOutputStream()` in PDFBox 3.x apply any filter automatically? In PDFBox 3.x, `createOutputStream()` creates a filter stream that applies the default filters. If the COSStream has a filter set before `createOutputStream()` is called, those filters are applied.

But in our code, we set the filter AFTER writing: `img.getCOSObject().setItem(COSName.FILTER, COSName.JBIG2_DECODE)` is called after the stream is written. And `createOutputStream()` is called without any prior filter setup (the PDImageXObject was just created with `new PDImageXObject(doc)`).

Wait, actually, `new PDImageXObject(doc)` internally calls something that might set up filters. Let me check PDFBox source...

Actually, I think the issue might be simpler. Let me check whether PDFBox applies a DEFLATE filter automatically. In PDFBox 3.x, when you create a stream with `createOutputStream()` and don't specify any filters, PDFBox applies DEFLATE compression by default (via the `COSWriter` during save). But `createOutputStream()` without arguments should just write raw bytes.

Actually, in PDFBox 3.x, `createOutputStream()` applies the default compression filter (which is usually FlateDecode) to the stream when it's saved. When we write raw JBIG2 data and then set `COSName.FILTER` to `COSName.JBIG2_DECODE`, we're telling the reader to use JBIG2 decoding. But PDFBox might ALSO apply FlateDecode on top!

If PDFBox wraps the JBIG2 data with FlateDecode, the actual PDF stream would be:
```
/Filter [/FlateDecode /JBIG2Decode]
```

This would DEFLATE the JBIG2 data, and then JBIG2 decode it. If the DEFLATE doesn't remove any data (lossless), the JBIG2 decoder would still see valid data. But if PDFBox stores it without FlateDecode on the image stream, then setting FILTER to JBIG2_DECODE should work.

Let me check the actual PDF stream dictionary for one of the JBIG2 images in output.pdf.

Actually, the `pdftoppm` errors tell me something important: "2709 extraneous bytes after segment" — this suggests the JBIG2 decoder reads all the way through the data and finds extra bytes at the end that don't form valid segments. 

BUT WAIT! What if the extra bytes are actually the DEFLATE headers/trailers? This would happen if PDFBox applied FlateDecode compression and the JBIG2 decoder tries to interpret the compressed data as JBIG2 segments!
